From savages at tvlinux.org  Wed Jun  1 05:28:26 2005
From: savages at tvlinux.org (Shaun Savage)
Date: Wed, 01 Jun 2005 05:28:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] apache2
In-Reply-To: <429D2EF5.6040601@xprt.net>
References: <429D2EF5.6040601@xprt.net>
Message-ID: <429DA9EA.50104@tvlinux.org>

Does your apache search  /etc/apache2/site-enabled/default for more  
config info?
I just leave the virtual host in the http.conf file for few sites but 
for large number of sites, I create a new directory  /etc/httpd/virtual/ 
and add domain.conf for each site.
then in httpd.conf add
include virtual/*.conf

If you are using hosts then only that machine will know the virtual 
sites. each domain would have to be added to the DNS for all machines to 
know where the IP of the virtual hosts is.

shaun

I went through this a couple months back, so I don't recall the details. 
The key is that there is a second place that you have to make entries in 
the httpd.conf file.

I don't have time to look right now because that machine is down stairs and 
I haven't looked at it in months. It just sits there and works, unlike the 
windows that I am writing this on.

I checked a copy of the httpd.conf on my pendrive, which may not be 
current, but it has the following:

### Section 3: Virtual Hosts
#
  . . . . . .  (left out stuff)
#
# Use name-based virtual hosting.
#
NameVirtualHost *:80

  . . . . . .  (left out stuff)

<VirtualHost *:80>
         ServerName 209.210.229.130
         DocumentRoot /var/www/html
         ServerSignature email
         DirectoryIndex  index.php index.html index.htm index.shtml
         LogLevel  warn
         HostNameLookups off
</VirtualHost>

# Virtual host Virtual Host 0
<VirtualHost *:80>
         ServerName www.schoppformetro.com
         ServerAlias schoppformetro.com
         DocumentRoot /var/www/w-schopp/html
         DirectoryIndex  index.php index.html index.htm index.shtml
</VirtualHost>

I think that all that only serverName, ServerAlias and DocumnetRoot are 
required.

My biggest problem getting it to work turned out to be permissions. (pay 
close attention to the content of error messages)

If this doesn't solve your problem, let me know & I will get you a copy of 
the whole file from the machine.

Good luck JK

In-Reply-To: <200505312235.j4VMZA607042@pop.hevanet.com>
References: <200505312235.j4VMZA607042@pop.hevanet.com>
Message-ID: <1117641459.4399.2.camel@differentiable>

On Tue, 2005-05-31 at 15:35 -0700, William A Morita wrote:
> Group,
> 
> I have put into /etc/fstab the line:
> 
> //server/datashare  /share smbfs   username=guest,rw  0 0
> 
> While this does mount the volume, the result is that non-root accounts do
> not have read/write access.

Bill, 

    I think you need something like: 

//server/datashare    /share    smbfs    username=guest,rw,gid=500    0
0

Where 500 is your common gid (users or something).

-josh

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Wed Jun  1 19:49:10 2005
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Wed, 1 Jun 2005 19:49:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] smbmount Queestion continued - Solved
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0505311552110.4280-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <200506020249.j522nB424249@pop.hevanet.com>

Group,

I finally got the result I was seeking: a wide open smb share mounted via
/etc/fstab .  Gid/uid of 99 is the "nobody" user, the same one that shows
when the smb volume is viewed on the server.  Users are added to group
"nobody" to get access.

//server/datashare  /share smbfs
guest,rw,gid=99,uid=99,dmask=775,fmask=775  0 0

- Bill Morita
wamorita at hevanet.com 



-----Original Message-----
From: Roderick A. Anderson [mailto:raanders at acm.org] 
Sent: Tuesday, May 31, 2005 4:11 PM
To: wamorita at hevanet.com; General Linux/UNIX discussion and help; civil and
on-topic
Subject: Re: [PLUG] smbmount Queestion continued

On Tue, 31 May 2005, William A Morita wrote:

> Group,
> 
> I have put into /etc/fstab the line:
> 
> //server/datashare  /share smbfs   username=guest,rw  0 0
> 
> While this does mount the volume, the result is that non-root accounts 
> do not have read/write access.

What are the permissions on /share?  Who are the user and group? What are
the permissions of //server/datashare on the Windows system?  Is there a
guest id ( with no password ) on the Windows system?

So after all those questions I'll supply some settings that ahve worked for
me ( YMMV ).

in /etc/fstab

//server/datashare /mount/point smbfs
credentials=/etc/samba/datashare,port=44

in /etc/samba/datashare

username=joeblow
password=xyzzy

/mount/point is joeblow:joeblow ug=rwx

The documentation indicates you may be able to set the group on /mount/point
to "users" and put all your users in that group and add an option the
gid=users to the fstab entry.


Rod
-- 
    "Open Source Software - You usually get more than you pay for..."
     "Build A Brighter Lamp :: Linux Apache {middleware} PostgreSQL"

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Wed Jun  1 21:38:00 2005
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Wed, 1 Jun 2005 21:38:00 -0700

On 6/1/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Anyone here using Qt for developing application GUIs? I'm trying to work
> through the designer tutorial (the Color Tool one) and find that I'm missing
> files that are supposed to be included. This is with the local qt-3.3.3
> installation.
> 
>    Are the other pieces available separately? I have what came with the
> Slackware-10.1 package.
> 

I did some development with QT 3.2.1.  The Distributions that I was working
with didn't seem to come with everything.  I ran head long into the same 
problem.  Everything seemed to work correctly except when I went and 
tried to follow the tutorials.  A couple of problems is that the online
docs and tutorials match up with the most current release.  These may
or may not use features that are only available with the new version.

My solution was to download a tar package from the trolltech web site and
build it.  Once I did that, everything work including getting the
tutorials working.
If you do build it, don't set for static libraries as some of the features of
the QT designer won't function correctly with the static libraries.

The install is more like follow the directions in the readme and install files.
There doesn't seem to be a true install of the package.

Here is the simplified install (from memory so beware)
unpack the tar in a location that you want it. 
point the necessary env-vars to that directory.
do ./configure in that dir.
do make and wait a long time ( I think it is longer than a kernel clean build)
symlink the dir into your /usr/lib dir replacing the existing qt dir.
put env-vars for the qt dir into your startup shell scripts.

For some reason I could not build all of the system as a user and have
it work (tried at least twice).  Had to build it as root.

Subject: [PLUG] UPS battery and Linux
Message-ID: <BAY20-F348A2B0644F7BE203BB7BCB8060@phx.gbl>


Dear All:

I have a rather stupid question hopefully someone can help me.

I never own a UPS battery till just weeks ago.  I finally
got around to install it with my Linux box.  It works fine.  The UPS
is nice enough to come with Linux driver and I managed to configure
it to talk to the UPS directly via serial link and SNMP.  I tested
that my linux box is gracefully shutdown after the UPS detected that power 
is lost.

I then noticed that ACPI isn't compiled into the kernel so I am in the 
process
of doing so.  For now, the graceful shutdown won't bring the whole box
to power shutdown.  It will just stop at "power down" message.
So someone has to power it down and power it back up when power restores.

My question is, even with ACPI installed and the box does power down
all the way,  someone will have to still power it back up later.  It is not 
obvious
to me if there is anyway the PC can power cycle itself after a power outage 
is over.
Without the UPS, the BIOS can reboot the box when power is back up.  This
won't allow for a graceful shutdown but at least the box is up whenever
power is back on.

Is there anyway, with UPS connected that my Linux box can boot up by
itself when power restores?

--Vincent

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Jun  2 13:55:11 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 2 Jun 2005 13:55:11 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] What I've Learned About Rekall
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506021350090.24665@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   After a four-hour exercise in frustration in trying to find and build
rekall (a dbms RAD described in the latest Linux Journal), I joined their
mail list and learned three interesting things:

   1.) Their web site is quite old and may or may not work with a particular
browser/version depending on ... the phase of the moon? Anyway, it's hit or
miss and not consistent.

   2.) If one wants to build a qt-only version, without KDE, one needs to
download the head of the cvs tree, run it through a pre-compilation filter
to remove the kde references, then build it. However, this version lags the
kde-specific version in terms of features and (apparently) bug fixes.

   3.) It's really a KDE application and not designed to be fully deployable
under any other desktop/window manager.

   Oh, well. Back to PyQt, Qt-designer, and papyrus.

Rich

Galen Seitz (galens at seitzassoc.com) typed this ...
> 
> I'm having problems trying to run mozilla on a remote Solaris 8 machine
> over a forwarded X11 connection.  If firefox is already running on my local
> machine and I try to run mozilla on the remote machine, another *local*
> firefox window comes up.  Here's the setup and an example:

Running X over an SSH tunnel is painful. What I do is run tinyproxy on
the remote server, then set up a tunnel to the machine running
tinyproxy. The browser runs on your local machine and has access to the
resources on the remote network over a secure connection. 

I'm assuming that the remote workstation is behind a firewall or at
least a NAT router. You probably don't want to be a proxy for the world.

> wizard:~$ /usr/dt/bin/mozilla &           # THIS OPENS A LOCAL FIREFOX WINDOW.

That's really odd.

> If I were just browsing the Web, this would be fine, but I need to view
> files which are only accessible to the remote machine.  What is the
> correct way to do this?  I assume this is happening because of my
> home directory automount.

You mean view local files in a browser? Or do you need to run an xterm
for some reason? The proxy trick works great if you want to view the
files in a browser.

From russj at dimstar.net  Thu Jun  2 16:50:09 2005
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Thu, 02 Jun 2005 16:50:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] UPS battery and Linux
In-Reply-To: <BAY20-F348A2B0644F7BE203BB7BCB8060@phx.gbl>
References: <BAY20-F348A2B0644F7BE203BB7BCB8060@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <429F9B31.5000607@dimstar.net>

Vincent Yau wrote:

>
> For now, the graceful shutdown won't bring the whole box
> to power shutdown.  It will just stop at "power down" message.
> So someone has to power it down and power it back up when power restores.

At that level, it's highly unlikely that any damage would result from 
the PC losing power, but it won't come back up when power returns.

>
> My question is, even with ACPI installed and the box does power down
> all the way,  someone will have to still power it back up later.  It 
> is not obvious
> to me if there is anyway the PC can power cycle itself after a power 
> outage is over.

In the BIOS, there is usually a setting that determines what happens 
when a system gets power back. If it's set to boot upon power 
restoration, then it will do so.

Here's the rub.

The UPS will continue to feed power to the system for a while. Could be 
a long while if nothing is draining it, and then power is restored.

So, you will want to have some other way to make the system come back 
up. Possibly wake on lan. Although, I can see pitfalls with that.

If your switch is powered by the UPS, then it won't lose power, and the 
system would come back up if any other systems on your net sends a 
broadcast packet...

If your switch isn't on the UPS, then any power glitches possibly knock 
you off the net while the switch resyncs... and your switch isn't 
protected by the UPS.

I believe most systems are set to only power down the system after a 
pre-determined amount of time has passed without power. That works well 
in our area, since most power outages are relatively short. At least, 
I've only had one outage last more than a minute in the 6 years I've 
been in Portland. So it might be worth it to find out how long your 
system will be able to be without power, and configure accordingly.

> Is there anyway, with UPS connected that my Linux box can boot up by
> itself when power restores?

Wake on LAN, wake on ring. Both work, both have other conciderations.

Russ

looks a *lot* like Access.

bothered to learn how to write applications or do reports, but I

> annoying in others.  I think the solution I found was to start with
> '-ProfileManager'.
> 

Thanks for the responses!

Wil's suggestion got me part way there.  

I created a second profile.  When I run "mozilla -P 2nd_profile", now I
get a mozilla window from my remote machine.  Unfortunately a second
invocation of the same command doesn't get me a second window, it brings
up the profile manager.  When I choose "2nd_profile", it refuses to run
because the profile is already in use.

To explain my setup a further, I have a Solaris machine on my local
network which I use to run EDA tools.  The Solaris machine is headless,
so I ssh to it and invoke the tools.  The GUI interface gets displayed
on my main Linux machine.  Occasionally the EDA tools will want to run a
browser, often for documentation or to check for software updates.  The
documentation lives on the Solaris machine, so starting a local browser
is not useful.  I could export the necessary directories and NFS mount them
locally, but I was trying to avoid that hassle.  The ultimate solution is
to get native Linux versions of the tools.  I think this is possible now,
but I haven't yet gone down that path.

For the curious, the tools are Mentor Graphics' ModelSim (Verilog simulator)
and Altera's Quartus (CPLD & FPGA design tool).


galen

From sean at harbours.us  Thu Jun  2 22:48:15 2005
From: sean at harbours.us (Sean Harbour)
Date: Thu, 2 Jun 2005 22:48:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] An alternate worst case data encryption recovery suggestion
In-Reply-To: <20050528171002.GA23448@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0505271644550.11498@sco302.DavidMandel.com>
	<86ll60wkz5.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<20050528171002.GA23448@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20050602224815.5ab570df.sean@harbours.us>

On Sat, 28 May 2005 10:10:02 -0700
Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:

> It might be helpful to set up some sort of "information escrow"
> mechanism for PLUG members. 

Your proposed method of requiring a majority of trusted key holders cooperate to recover an individuals private data is a good suggestion, but would require significant planning and coordination among the involved parties. It could possibly be more hassle than the average code warrior would want to deal with.

As a different alternative, I remember seeing an article a few years ago describing some sort of time based expiration of encryption keys. I can't find it on google, of course, but from memory the gist was a trusted source would distribute a set of encryption keys that were time stamped. You would separately encrypt the information with the dated key for when you would want that info available in case of loss of your private key due to death, imprisonment, whatever. At regular intervals, the trusted source would publish the decryption keys as each date occurred.

It would most likely be implemented in a similiar fashion to current SSL keys authentication, that is, everyone would have to agree on a trusted organization to house the master keys and distribute updates. And there would be a mechanism in place to automatically check for the latest decryption keys.

If it worked as planned, you could regularly make a backup of your information and encrypt it with a public date key. Every month or year, delete the old backup and re-encrypt. In a worst case scenario, whoever has access to your physical storage will be able to decrypt it at some pre-determined date in the future, but no sooner. The downside is that you would have to keep strict control of any such encrypted data. Any copies outside your control would be wide open after the specified date. It would make a great way to publish whistleblower letters to the public domain, however.

The author of the article was also touting the possibility of integrating the system with the electronic banking system, bringing up the possibility of writing post-dated electronic money transfers, if you will. Interesting idea, and I'll bet there is some startup company working on it right now.

Does anybody have a link to the original article or, better yet, a working example?

Thanks,

Sean Harbour

I am using apache2..

It does not use httpd.

/etc/apache2/sites-enabled/default

and I use IP not :80

Worked great..

> client.

>From the FAQ:

  A4. How can I prevent the use of NFS Version 2, or of other NFS versions?

    A. The protocol version is determined at mount time, and can be
    modified by specifying the version of the NFS protocol, or the
    version of the transport protocol, supported by the server. For
    example, the client mount command

    mount -o vers=3 foo:/ /bar will request that the server use NFS
    Version 3 when granting a mount request (Note that "vers" and
    "nfsvers" have the same meaning in the mount command; The string
    "vers" is compatible with NFS implementations on Solaris and other
    vendors). If you wish to prevent use of NFS Version 2 in all cases,
    then you must restart rpc.mountd on the server, with the option "-N
    1 -N 2". The best way to do this is to modify the nfs rpc.mountd
    configuration on the server by modifying the NFS startup script
    options, and then shutting down and restarting NFS as a whole:

        * cd /etc/rc.d/init.d
        * Modify RPCMOUNTDOPTS in the nfs script to include "-N 1 -N 2"
        * Restart nfs (you must have root access) with "./nfs restart"

    You will now get the following error when attemping to nfs mount a
    file system using NFS Version 2 (now unrecognized) after restarting
    rpc.mountd:

    mount: RPC: Unable to receive; errno = Connection refused

    You will also subsequently get the following (non-fatal) warning
    when you unmount any nfs mounted file system at all, regardless of
    when it was mounted:

    Bad UMNT RPC: RPC: Program/version mismatch; low version = 3, high version = 3



Once you've done the mount, I think you should be able to confirm the
version by watching the stats change in the nfsstats report.

galen


From eli.stair at gmail.com  Sat Jun  4 09:25:54 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Sat, 4 Jun 2005 10:25:54 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Problem nfs-mounting "large files"
In-Reply-To: <20050604145131.4FF838F10@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20050604030634.3CE7B198C8@medulla.enet.sharplabs.com>
	<20050604145131.4FF838F10@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae050604092523e5abf6@mail.gmail.com>

Unfortunately I've found some nasty bugs in RH and others' mountd/nfsd
parsing,  even if you specify a no-version "-N whatever", it still
usually gets advertised and can still be used.  Linux's
nfsutils/util-linux mount doesn't always do what you tell it properly
either, when conneting to some NFS servers (NetApps), it usually takes
the server's preferred mount, and will not error and fail even if you
specify something different.

To drop support from the daemon runtime, do a:

rpcinfo -d mountd 2
rpcinfo -d nfsd 2

When issuing your mount from the client, do:

mount -o nfsvers=3,tcp <...whatever...>

If that is indeed your problem, it will allow you to test it.  I don't
 see the connection between the underlying filesystem size and any
filesize handling issue, after all you're not exporting a 4GB file,
you're exporting a filesystem.  I'm assuming you can properly see the
contents of the loopback mount locally on the box?

/eli

From mikeraz at patch.com  Sun Jun  5 04:40:01 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Sun, 5 Jun 2005 04:40:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] apache2
In-Reply-To: <429D2EF5.6040601@xprt.net>
References: <429D2EF5.6040601@xprt.net>
Message-ID: <20050605114001.GC5884@patch.com>

Vram wrote:
> Hi
> 
> I  am trying to set up Multiple Domains with one IP...
> 
> I am using apache2...
> 
> And I followed the example that come with the doc....
> 
> It dosen;t seen to work

In my out-of-the-box Apache 2 httpd.conf the section on NameVirtualHost
reads:

   NameVirtualHost *

   # All VirtualHost definitions are in seperate files
   include /etc/httpd/conf/vhost.d

If yours does also I highly suggest going with the flow and creating
individual virtual host definition files in that directory[1]

This is _very_ easy to work with and adding additional named virtual
hosts is as easy as adding a file with contents similar to:

  <VirtualHost  *>
        ServerName dawg.patch.com
        ServerAdmin dawgadmin at patch.com
        DocumentRoot /var/www/dawg
        ScriptAlias /cgi-bin/ /var/www/cgi-bin/dawg/
        CustomLog /var/log/httpd/dawg-access.log combined
        ErrorLog /var/log/httpd/dawg-error.log
  </VirtualHost>

Then running the init script for apache with a "reload" parameter.


[1] Or creating another directory and identifying it at this point 
in the config file.

From alan at clueserver.org  Sun Jun  5 19:28:17 2005
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Sun, 05 Jun 2005 19:28:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] problem mounting a Zip Drive
In-Reply-To: <200506041708.12688.t_rex@verizon.net>
References: <200506041708.12688.t_rex@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <1118024898.9912.4.camel@dagon.fnordora.org>

On Sat, 2005-06-04 at 17:08 -0700, richard cowger wrote:
> I am trying to mount a 250Meg Zip Drive to my SUSE 9.2 system.
> The Bios can "see" the drive
> I followed the Iomega recommended  procedure, but  Linux still doesn't  see 
> the drive.
> 
> Anyone know how to mount a Zip drive on a Linux system.

Zip drives are strange.  Try this:

Create a /mnt/zip directory.

If the drive is an ide on /dev/hdb, then

  mount /dev/hdb4 /mnt/zip

The zip drive acts like a device with scsi id 4, but through IDE.  Makes
for a strange mount point.

Our developers make changes in a development environment.  Before
those changes are moved to production, their manager approves the
change.  Then the requested change is forwarded to the Server Admin
group to implement.  Sometimes the change is as simple as "Install
this RPM" and sometimes it involves a lot of SQL with numerous steps
done in a particular order for table changes, etc.  We want a better
way for the developer to make this request, for the manager to read
and approve this request, and for the admin to then implement the
request.  Later on if we discover a problem with a system, then we'd
like to search through the changes to narrow down which change most
likely caused the new problems.
Thanks,
~M

Message-ID: <20050606223446.GO15495@bavariati.org>

On Mon, Jun 06, 2005 at 12:46:24PM -0700, Matt Alexander wrote:
> I'm looking for an Open Source app for our in-house developers to use
> for submitting changes to the Server Admins such as upgrading a
> package, modifying config files, running SQL commands, etc.  Managers
> would need to be in the loop for approving these changes before the
> Admins see them.  Being able to search a history of changes per system
> would be needed too.

Sounds a lot like a job-ticket system.  Request Tracker is my favourite.

In this case, devs would either email requests to RT or create tickets
in the web interface.  If managers need to approve, let them take
requests from the incoming ticket queue and either deny each ticket or
assign it to a tech (or even to an "approved" ticket queue.)

Heck, you could give each manager or department its own "approval"
queue, with its own email address.

RT is nice because folks get a decent amount of email feedback about how
their request is being handled, without it being overwhelming.
It's also pretty configurable, with permissions, custom fields and such.

I've found a ticket system to be essential.  Just make sure someone is
repsonsible for making sure tickets don't back up in the queue or get
stale.  We did this on a rotating basis at OSDL.

Actually, I'm kinda surprised RT hasn't caught on in other areas
besides IT.  It's a great way to keep track of who's handling what,
and how it's all going.

Maybe if it cost $5000 per seat and only ran on AS/400,
  Aaron

From aaron at bavariati.org  Mon Jun  6 17:44:09 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)

On Mon, Jun 06, 2005 at 04:00:16PM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Mon, 6 Jun 2005, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> >Sounds a lot like a job-ticket system.  Request Tracker is my favourite.
> 
>   Good call. RT is used by the GRASS development team to record bugs and
> track progress on squashing them.

Cool!  It's great software.

> >Actually, I'm kinda surprised RT hasn't caught on in other areas besides
> >IT. It's a great way to keep track of who's handling what, and how it's all
> >going.
> 
>   Is F/OSS application development considered IT?

Sure.  But while RT can act kinda like a bug-tracking system,
bug-trackers and trouble-ticket systems are rather different,
close-up.  A robust bug-tracker deals with workflow, versioning and
permissions issues that a trouble-ticket system should never see.

But most orgs don't need all that folderol.  The ones that do need it
get proprietary and/or custom stuff, or put up with Bugzilla.

Just underclock the P4 so that its performance rating matches that of a 
laptop-capable G4. Heat worries will disappear! :-)

More to the point, IBM seemingly didn't want to throw its weight behind 
the development of a serious mobile CPU for general-purpose computing. 
My reading is that Apple is all about mobility: the G5 desktops and 
Xserve units are sexy, but it's the laptops that are selling OS X these 
days. If PowerPC wasn't going to make a serious play in the 
mobile-performance category, then Apple did the right thing.

> couldn't come up with a 64-bit chip suitable for Apple laptops. So I
> would imagine you'll see an x86-64 PowerBook.

...Intel being a subset of x86 these days.  Sure, they have the Itanium
II and the Xeon EMT64 chips, but their consumer-grade chips are all x86.

If Apple wanted to move on in to the 64-bit world, the clear choice
would be AMD, with affordable and well-tested x86-64 chips... ones that
actually go in laptops.  :-)

I don't personally think we'll see an x86-64 PowerBook.

From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Jun  7 16:32:23 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Tue, 7 Jun 2005 16:32:23 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Debian 3.1 (Sarge) Released
In-Reply-To: <20050607190003.AC83D43A3@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200506072332.j57NWNn6020123@m5p.com>

>From: "Steve Bonds" <1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com>
> On 6/7/05, Galen Seitz wrote:
> > I'll have to assume you've never programmed an x86 at the assembly
> > level.  It is indeed a very very cold day in hell.
> 
> I think you meant to say:
> 
> Have I'll assume to never you've an programmed x86 an the at level assembly.

That is the smallest of all the x86 offenses. The PowerPC mnemonics all
have the destination register as the first one. x86's problems are the
mere 8 sort of general-purpose registers and just plain overall bizzare
design. Notably multiply instructions always use EAX and EDX. Don't get
me started on the SSE vector unit, that is *really* *bizzare*. They
designed the MMX unit 4 years earlier, which was decent within its
limitations; then they produce the SSE unit which is *horrific*. WTH were
they thinking?!

PowerPC has 31 truely general-purpose registers, and is just plain very
well designed. I can easily do a huge number of things with the PPC
vector unit (while SSE is useless except for a few things it was
deliberately designed for). Doesn't have conditional moves, but because
instructions don't have to write the status register, spacing branches
far away from the condition is easy. The multiply-add instructions are
nice too (even has vector multiply-add).

The suggestion that Intel will make chips that conform to the PPC
specification is kind of wild, but you never know. Note that they're
already doing three architectures, ia32 (soon the amd64 extensions too),
ARM and ia64. Adding a fourth would be a little wild, but they've
certainly got the ability. Apple would be crazy to go for a non-64bit
chip, this means either amd64 or PPC (ia64 isn't appropriate for
laptops).

I'm surprised not to find more discussion along these lines here.

I personally don't care about "Linux desktop vendors" (whoever THEY are), 
but I certainly think that this is more about getting OSX running on 
everybody's home PC.  Everybody knows Apple has a better interface. 
Everybody knows that Microsoft sucks ass.  And since iTunes is such a big 
hit, folks are looking more and more toward Apple.  (I have thought 
recently that iTunes was intentionally not rolling out an integrated video 
player so that Microsoft wouldn't immediately target them as a threat. 
Already, folks prefer it to Windows Media Player for their music.  It'll 
be doing movies soon and then it'll be on everyone's desktop... the OS is 
next.)

But they have hardware... and they have a kind of investment.  There's a 
sort of religion out there where folks say, "I'm a PC guy... Macs are cool 
and all, but too sleek and hermetic."

I think they're pretending this is about changing chipsets and it's really 
about taking down Microsoft.

I don't really like the idea of one piece of proprietary software 
replacing another, but at least it's unix and has a chance of being 
useful!

Cheap hardware and OSX is probably exactly what Microsoft fears most.

From cstevens at gencom.us  Tue Jun  7 23:05:23 2005
From: cstevens at gencom.us (D. Cooper Stevenson)
Date: Tue, 07 Jun 2005 23:05:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Command Line Switches from Web Page?
In-Reply-To: <42A66F80.6070202@axpr.net>
References: <1118190675.20406.76.camel@sparticas>
	<1118200530.15363.23.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>
	<42A66F80.6070202@axpr.net>
Message-ID: <1118210723.20406.85.camel@sparticas>

On Tue, 2005-06-07 at 21:09 -0700, AthlonRob wrote:

> Kenneth G. Stephens wrote:
> I think Cooper is looking to run code on the client machines, not the
> servers... if I'm not mistaken.


Right. The challenge is that the application I wish to run uses a
peer-to-peer networking paradigm, not client/server. That means that I
have to find a way to join multiple remote computers together from a
central location.

Think of it like an old switchboard, who's operator receives an incoming
connection, asks for the connection type (destination IP, port number,
etc. to mix the metaphor), and then rings the opposite end.

In this case, the "ringing" is starting a client on each end via an RPC
call to the remote computers; one will have the --host switch while the
other gets the --join switch.

After the central server has sent the RPC calls to the remote machines,
it's out of the loop-the two peers now communicate directly.

Of course I'll do this via a secure line but that's not the nut I'm
trying to crack at the moment.


-Coop

> it's out of the loop-the two peers now communicate directly.
> 

Then something like the method that the voip people are doing should work
for you.

Nope. Desktop chips only, and worse *not* *all* desktop chips. Looks like

too difficult actually if you keep your code endian-safe, and confine
your hardware-specific bits carefully.

I have been playing with CUPS, considering converting from BSD lpd.
This is on Debian.  I can print locally, from the host with the
printer.  I have the server's /etc/cups/cupsd.conf configured to
listen on an inside interface, but a client host on that network isn't
able to print anything.  I get a message in the the client's
/var/log/cups/error_log "Unable to get printer status
(client-error-forbidden)!"  On the server's /var/log/cups/error_log I
get '"POST /printers/dj HTTP/1.1" 403 0'.  I presume I need to add
something to the server's /etc/cups/cupsd.conf to grant access to the
host, but I am not sure exactly what/where.  Google has been
recalcitrant.  CUPS docs are diffuse.  Hints?

From guy1656 at ados.com  Wed Jun  8 07:00:11 2005
From: guy1656 at ados.com (GLL)
Date: Wed, 8 Jun 2005 07:00:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] problem mounting a Zip Drive
In-Reply-To: <200506041708.12688.t_rex@verizon.net>
References: <200506041708.12688.t_rex@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <200506080700.11367.guy1656@ados.com>


: I followed the Iomega recommended  procedure, but  Linux still doesn't  see
: the drive.
:
: Anyone know how to mount a Zip drive on a Linux system.
:
: Rich Cowger
: t_rex at verizon.net

Good question - I am a 'low-level' user and don't know of any Linux systems 
that really 'play' the USB game right, as in: You fire up your computer, and 
'whatever' you plug into that USB port pretty much works because the USB 
standard for interrogating what's just been plugged in is understood. Maybe 
we just need to wait until a later rev comes out that's fully plug-n-play for 
USB, but that's been like soccer fans waiting for soccer to get 'big' in the 
USA.

I am running Mandrake 10.0 right now, and while the system sometimes 
recognizes new things plugged into the USB port, (an icon appears on the 
desktop) it doesn't let you DO anything with them automatically. You probably 
have to open a commend shell and type in a bunch of cryptic crap, and you 
could search the Internet for DAYS and still not find a good explanatory 
site.

Your question has gone unanswered here for days because for the people who 
know how to answer it - it's too simple to bother with, and they are off 
discussing topics which are intellectually challenging to THEM - after all 
this is all a volunteer effort, so you do what you like and you don't have to 
do what you don't like. Linux programs will always have poorer UI's than Win 
or Mac becuase for the people who know how to program, the fun is in the 
programming, and once that's solved they're off discovering the core of next 
fun program - Porting the solved program out to the rest of the world is 
drudgery, so it often doesn't get done well in Linux, if at all. 

For now, I have an Iomega Zip drive and an external ZIP H/D kit with a 200-gig 
disk mounted inside. I cannot get Linux to plug-n-play the IOmega, and I 
haven't even tried the 200GB.

I used to cheat with a Win2K installation running under VMWare, plus a shared 
directory: Fire up VMWare, and plug that USB thingie in and 'voila' it works 
- then you can transfer the data into your shared directory and Linux can see 
it.

Another program you might try is puTTY - You can network a Win plug-n-play 
system to your Linux box and actually transfer files with one command from 
the Win-box.

- GLL

Where Speed & Service Have Always Mattered @ http://www.ados.com

http://www.radioshack.com/product.asp?catalog%5Fname=CTLG&category%5Fname=CTLG%5F011%5F006%5F002%5F000&product%5Fid=276%2D143

"High output infrared LED", available at most area stores.
Note the forward voltage of 1.28V.  Prolly run forever on an AA
battery, though if you need very consistent intensity, use 2 AAs and
have one'a your EEs whip up a current-mirror to drive it.

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Wed Jun  8 23:08:27 2005
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Wed, 8 Jun 2005 23:08:27 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] problem mounting a Zip Drive
In-Reply-To: <200506080700.11367.guy1656@ados.com>
Message-ID: <20050609060827.1948.qmail@web50607.mail.yahoo.com>



--- GLL <guy1656 at ados.com> wrote:

> 
> : I followed the Iomega recommended  procedure, but 
> Linux still doesn't  see
> : the drive.
> :
> : Anyone know how to mount a Zip drive on a Linux
> system.
> :
> : Rich Cowger
> : t_rex at verizon.net
> 
> Good question - I am a 'low-level' user and don't
> know of any Linux systems 
> that really 'play' the USB game right, as in: You
> fire up your computer, and 
> 'whatever' you plug into that USB port pretty much
> works because the USB 
> standard for interrogating what's just been plugged
> in is understood. 

I hope someone comes up with a better answer than
mine. Anyhow, the device name is usually /dev/sda1 or
/dev/sdb1 for usb devices, if it's the first one. A
mount command, which ordinarily requires root
permission, would be something like this:

mount -t ext3 /dev/sda1 /mnt/zip

If your filesystem is ext3; otherwise it's different.
Also you may need to create the mount point. It
doesn't have to be /mnt/zip, so long as you don't try
to mount it on a filesystem that's already occupied.

Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Jun  9 10:33:38 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Thu, 9 Jun 2005 10:33:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu 64-bit DVD
Message-ID: <42A81B02.32178.320556EE@localhost>

My new Compaq R3240 (Athlon 64, NVidia chip) arrived on Monday 
and I have been playing with it, trying one distro after another to 
see which ones did the best at finding and configuring hardware. I 
started with the live CD I had of Ubuntu and was highly pleased. 
The screen came up at 1680 x 1050! Stunning! If anyone recalls, 
dfficulties with the ATI video in the R4000 were the reason I 
returned it to HP.

However, I wanted to use the live CD to transfer the Windows XP 
Home files to my external USB drive for "just in case," and then 
just format the whole hard drive for Linux. And Ubuntu live couldn't 
see the hard drive. I also tried the Knoppix live CD. It came up in 
only VESA 1024 x 768, but it could see the hard drive. 
Unfortunately, it wouldn't copy the files to the USB drive. All I got 
was error messages. So eventually I just booted Windows, 
installed BackupMYPC, and made a backup to the USB drive.

Then I installed Linux on the whole hard drive. I started with Fedora 
Core 3 from the DVD that came in the "unleashed" book I got at 
Powell's. But too many problems, so I tried Mandriva 2005 LE, from 
the DVD that I downloaded from MandrivaClub. Again, too many 
problems. I left Suse for the last. It did pretty good, but still only 
VESA 1024 x 768. I lusted for that beautiful Ubuntu video!

I had the Ubuntu CDs that were handed out at the last meeting. I 
used the install CD and it went all the way through. But when I 
rebooted I got a kernel panic -- something about APIC that I didn't 
understand. But I had a hunch it might be because that CD is the 
32-bit version. So I downloaded the 64-bit CD from Ubuntu. 
Success!! And the most gorgeous screen I've ever seen on a 
laptop! I'm serious. Text is so sharp and crisp it looks like a laser 
printout. The letters are only about an eighth of an inch high, but I 
can read them clearly from five feet distance. This thing rocks! 

Not only that, Ubuntu did better than Suse at other things. Suse 
found my network and cable modem, but could never see my 
Windows 2000 computer. With Ubuntu I clicked on the network, it 
popped up with "Windows Network." I clicked on that, and it then 
found "Workgroup" (which is what I called it on the Windows 2000 
computer). And when I clicked on Workgroup, there was the D:\ 
drive on my Windows 2000 computer, that I had previously created 
a share for. I clicked on the D:\ drive, it asked for my password, I 
entered it, and there were all my files!

So now I'm in love with Ubuntu. But I want the whole thing, not just 
the cut-down stuff that is on the CD. So now I am downloading the 
64-bit DVD. Unfortunately, the only way to do so is with BitTorrent. 
It's been almost two full days and still has 16.5 hours to go. Ubuntu 
will send you free CDs, but not the DVDs. :(

But once I have it, I'd be happy to burn a DVD for anyone who 
wants one. Note that this is the 64-bit DVD, and the install one, not 
the live one.

OK, now I'm off to cafepress.com to order an Ubuntu t-shirt. :)

From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Thu Jun  9 21:52:57 2005
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Thu, 09 Jun 2005 21:52:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Website hosting at home
In-Reply-To: <42A912E0.3060507@parkrose.k12.or.us>
References: <1118356050.8874.30.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Thu, 2005-06-09 at 21:11 -0700, Dave Poirier wrote:
> Just came across...
> http://zoneedit.com/
> "free DNS and domain management for the first 5 domains"
> Thought it could be useful for someone. I personally have never used
> it.  Anyone out there have any experience with them?

I don't have experience with them, but I am very pleased with the
service at http://freedns.afraid.org/ 

I host 6 domains there, two of which are active. The service covers any
number of domains, is very easy to use, and supports easy dynamic
updates for those uncomfortable times when your IP address changes. You
can have almost any type of DNS record, including TXT records for SPF.
And there is no advertising, anywhere. I bought a premium account
($30/year for the lowest level) to support the operation and to get
wildcard entries, but if your needs are modest there's no reason you
would need to use anything but the free service.

I highly recommend it.

Evan

From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Thu Jun  9 23:18:50 2005
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Thu, 09 Jun 2005 23:18:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Digital photo management software
Message-ID: <1118384330.3145.46.camel@localhost.localdomain>

Hi all,

I have a collection of almost 800 photographs taken with my digital
camera, and I'm always taking more. Each image is around 1 MB, but my
new camera produces JPG's betweeen 2 and 4 MB.

I am looking for a robust, reliable, and easy to use application that
allows me to do these things:

      * Browse by category or group, not by file location on disk.
      * Browse by thumbnail, not filename.
      * Assign an image to more than one category.
      * Create and manage a hierarchy of categories.
      * View a set of images (sequentially) at large size, full screen,
        and/or 1:1 scaling.
      * Add comments or descriptions to images.

It would be nice if it supports:

      * EXIF and/or IPTC data.
      * Canon RAW format (.cr2, which "file" identifies as TIFF but
        isn't really) in files around 8 MB.
      * Export capability for the metadata, or at least a
        well-documented database format so I can write my own export
        utilities.

I expect it would be a local X11 application, but I'm not opposed to a
web app if it's smooth and quick. I only post a small subset of my
photos online, and I already have a system for that.

I have tried searching for something like this, but I can't think of
what this software might be called. If you can't recommend a specific
application, can you think of what I can search for?

Thanks for your help,
Evan

P.S. Don't hesitate to mention apps that are close, but a bit rough
around the edges. This is for me, not my technophobic mother. :-)

Don't forget to make sure that you can tag images in groups, such as 
when importing a bunch of pictures to the same category off of the 
camera.

So far, it seems like too many of these programs want to store the files 
in their own special way.  The trouble is that if you can't do 
everything that every other program can do, what you really need is a 
way to easily use multiple programs in the same system.

That's why I mostly use gqview.  It has a nice hook system for running 
commands on multiple images.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Jun 10 14:16:37 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 10 Jun 2005 14:16:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] case-sensitive cvs on Mac OS X
Message-ID: <42AA0335.8090200@madboa.com>

Here's an obscure one I figured I ought to post for Google's sake:

Macintoshes historically have had case-insensitive filesystems. That 
remains the default with OS X, but you can create case-sensitive 
filesystems, if you so choose. (The Mac mailing lists are full of funny 
posts asking why anyone would case about case sensitivity. Well, they're 
funny to me anyway. :-)

Our main file server at work is an Xserve; the system disk is formatted 
in normal Mac fashion, but the export volume is case-sensitive. So, if 
you login to that box, $HOME is case-sensitive, but /var/tmp is not.

Local requirements recently led me to compile and install a newer 
version of cvs than ships with 10.3.9. Lacking any firm direction in the 
matter, the cvs configure script automagically decides whether or not to 
build a case-sensitive binary based on the build-time filesystem. I 
built cvs in /var/tmp, so the binary was case-insensitive, but the 
repository is on the exported, case-sensitive filesystem. Oy!

The bottom line: When building cvs on OS X systems, make sure you know 
which sort of filesystem will be holding your repositories. Either build 
it on the target filesystem or make sure you know whether to pass the 
--enable-case-sensitivity (or --disable-case-sensitivity) option to the 
configure script.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

If I were running a commercial site, I'd want a static IP.  But if I
were running a commercial site, I'd use a colo service or rent a server
somewhere.  I think Mr. Leake's site is less mission critical, so he can
most likely get away with just using his given IP.  Most people do not
turn off their modems very often, so the IP is very unlikely to change.

From fixin at peak.org  Fri Jun 10 16:30:06 2005
From: fixin at peak.org (Eric House)
Date: Fri, 10 Jun 2005 16:30:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Website hosting at home
In-Reply-To: <20050610222639.DFA24447A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050610222639.DFA24447A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20050610233006.GC32101@peak.org>

> If I want them to assign a static IP address to me, I do think I
> must get a business license from them. I'm assuming that if you want
> to host a web site you need static IP address.

This isn't true.  You can use a free service like zoneedit or dyndns
to bind a dynamic IP address to a domain name that you own.  Use
something like ez-ipupdate to update the binding every time you're
assigned a new IP address.

I've been doing this for years, and hosting low-volume websites
(though not on port 80, which might be why nobody's complained).  I
think that as long as you're a paying customer and your bandwidth
usage is below the point at which your broadband provider wants to be
rid of you they'll leave you alone.  I can't see a good business
reason for them not to.  Just don't wind up on slashdot.

The DNS-binding process triggered by ez-ipupdate is pretty quick.  On
one machine I have a script, run every five minutes, that compares the
current IP address assigned to my DSL modem with the address dig finds
for my domain name.  When they're different it calls ez-ipupdate.
>From the logs it seems that it never runs twice in a row: dig starts
returning the new address before five minutes have passed.

In all, I see no reason to pay extra for a static IP address.

--Eric
--

>http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

HI

It is a long story.

Ubuntu believes in SUDO..

And there is no activated root..

To make root do this

SUDO passwd root
yada  yada

So you enter you sudo passwd <your password you are sudo>
Then enter and reenter the new root password.


Now you can use


su


Good luck

HTH

Vram...




From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Fri Jun 10 18:08:57 2005
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Fri, 10 Jun 2005 18:08:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Website hosting at home
In-Reply-To: <005401c56e0f$5ccfe360$6401a8c0@beachparty>
References: <005401c56e0f$5ccfe360$6401a8c0@beachparty>
Message-ID: <1118452137.4507.9.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Fri, 2005-06-10 at 15:54 -0700, Ted Kubaska wrote:
> I don't know. I'm not hosting but friends (non-Linux BTW) that do always
> arrange to get a static IP. Are there performance or reliability issues with
> DDNS?

There will be a period of time when your site will be inaccessible
because the DNS still points to the old IP. If your DNS provider allows
a very short TTL (and all other DNS servers respect it) and you have a
good way to detect a change of IP address, this delay can be less than 5
minutes.

Static IP's are much easier to deal with. No fussing, no worrying.

Evan

From dwight at dwightandamy.com  Sat Jun 11 21:59:53 2005

Subject: [PLUG] Time to replace firewall/mail/web/dns/etc .. suggestions ?
In-Reply-To: <1118528091.6233.60.camel@l1.pdxeng.com>
References: <CAENLEIHEHPLANCLMFKMCEDGEPAA.mwlist@planetwills.com>
	<1118528091.6233.60.camel@l1.pdxeng.com>
Message-ID: <0EE7CD7C-E488-4119-8131-3A3E37926B52@dwightandamy.com>

Linksys WRT54Gs are cheap (cost around $60, although I saw them on  
sale recently for $45), small, low power and run Linux.  The source  
code for the WRT54G is available and there are several custom distros  
for them.  They make decent firewall/vpn/dns/ids/wap/managed switch.   
The main drawback is there's no disk so they are essentially useless  
as mail/web servers.

I know nothing of Linux yet, but I had a Laserjet 4 for years. 
Indestructible printers. They use the cheapest toner, too. That's 
because they were made by Canon and HP just rebadged them, 
and then sold bazillions of them.

But instead of a 4 I'd get a 4M+. It's the same printer (same toner), 
but 12 ppm instead of 8, and has PostScript (genuine Adobe). And 
the 4+ can take a duplexer, which the 4 cannot.

And the cheapest place to get them (and toner) is eBay.

On Sun, 12 Jun 2005 09:39:58 -0700 Michael D. Harris wrote:

> Okay, I am at the point where I would like to use a printer with Linux.  I 
> have not done this yet and would like to make it as simple as possible.  So, 
> what printers do you use?  Do you have a favorite that was easy to set up?
> 
I'll second the observations that HP printers are well supported under
Linux but HP printer toner/ink supplies are expensive.

Do you need just a black and white printer or do you need a full color
printer?  If the former, any later model LaserJet printer should work.

If you need a color printer, you might look at one of the HP all-in-one
printer/fax/copier/scanner models.  I believe a model currently widely
available is the HP OfficeJet 4215 -- I got one only a few months ago,
for about $160 including a set of full-size replacement ink cartridges.

Anticipated printing volume should also influence your decision.  If you
plan to do a lot of business printing, you definitely want the LaserJet,
since the all-in-one printers are better suited to low volume workloads.

> Okay, I am at the point where I would like to use a printer with Linux.  I 
> have not done this yet and would like to make it as simple as possible.  So, 
> what printers do you use?  Do you have a favorite that was easy to set up?
> 
> Mike
 
Rising from the dead, Carla writes:

Check out  http://linuxprinting.org/. There are a lot of color and laserjet 
printers that work well under Linux, and this site has a database showing 
which ones are best-supported, and has a lot of good information.

Even though it is often cursed to the heavens, CUPS is a very nice printing 
system. Much better than the old line-printer protocols. The CUPS web 
interface rather sux, but you can also use KDEPrint or Gnome-Print, which 
many folks prefer. Make sure you download both the foomatic and gimp-print 
driver packages, and ESP Ghostscript. Having those will prevent 99% of Linux 
printing problems. The Gimp-print drivers are usually the best.

From aschlemm at comcast.net  Sun Jun 12 23:09:29 2005
From: aschlemm at comcast.net (Tony Schlemmer)
Date: Sun, 12 Jun 2005 23:09:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] printer preference?
In-Reply-To: <200506120939.59281.michael.harrisdmd@comcast.net>
References: <200506120939.59281.michael.harrisdmd@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200506122309.30158.aschlemm@comcast.net>

On Sunday 12 June 2005 09:39, Michael D. Harris wrote:
> Okay, I am at the point where I would like to use a printer with Linux.  I
> have not done this yet and would like to make it as simple as possible. 
> So, what printers do you use?  Do you have a favorite that was easy to set
> up?
>
> Mike

I've used several different HP Laserjet printers with Linux over the years. I 
still have a HP 4P that I've had for nearly 11 years and it still works great 
other than it prints 4 pages per minute. I also have a newer HP1300 that also 
works fine with Linux. Supports both PCL and Postscript and with modern Linux 
distros using CUPS it's pretty easy to get a printer working with Linux.

I've for my old 4P on a parallel port and my newer HP1300 is on the network 
using a little network print server box from Hawking Technology that allows 
me to share the printer on a network. I went barebones on the 1300 and got it 
in a non-network configuration and so it only has parallel port or USB 
available for a connection. My network print server channels the network data 
into a USB port for the printer. There are also parallel port versions of the 
printer server as well.

When looking for a printer you might want to checkout the Linux printer page 
at: http://www.linuxprinting.org. There's good information available on the 
site about wheather a particular model of printer is support under Linux.

Tony

-- 
Anthony Schlemmer
aschlemm at comcast.net

Subject: [PLUG] BASH Busted??
Message-ID: <200506140206.j5E26b393503@pop.hevanet.com>

Group,
 
According to the documentation on BASH, the shell variables are Global with
respect to functions.  To have a "local" in a function, it must be declared
as such.  This is consistent with KSH.  However, the following script does
not seem to demonstrate this
 
#!/bin/bash
# Script to test "global" variable 
#
 
set -x
 
JUNK="Initial Value"
 
function SomeFunction
{
  set -x
  JUNK="Set in function"
  echo $JUNK
}
 
Value="`SomeFunction`"
#Num=$((SomeFunction))
#let Num=SomeFunction
 
echo "Value=" $Value
echo "JUNK =" "$JUNK"
 


Did I mis-read to documentation, or is BASH busted?

The backticks are messing you up, they're running SomeFunction in an
entirely seperate process.  Try:

  SomeFunction
  Value=$?

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Jun 14 15:10:43 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 14 Jun 2005 15:10:43 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Gentoo Founder Moves to Redmond
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506141509090.22942@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

                      Linux distro daddy goes to Redmond
                                By Gavin Clarke
                  Published Tuesday 14th June 2005 14:26 GMT
                         <http://www.theregister.com/>

Microsoft has recruited the father of a popular Linux distribution, under a
continuing mission to further understand ? some would argue "extinguish" ?
the open source operating system. Daniel Robbins, founder and chief architect
of the Gentoo distribution, is moving to Redmond to help Microsoft
"understand open source and community?based projects" according to OSNews.

Robbins' recruitment comes as Microsoft makes increasingly friendly noises
towards Linux. According to reports, Red Hat chief executive and prez Matthew
Szulik has been courted by Microsoft and even joined chief software architect
Bill Gates for lunch at an exclusive New York eaterie earlier this year.

Linux fans will likely suspect the latest moves are another example of what
critics say is Microsoft's "embrace, extend, extinguish" strategy.

In this case, that could mean taking elements from Linux and open source that
help advance the Windows architecture and ecosystem of developers, rather
than doing something radical like build a version of Microsoft Office for,
say, Red Hat.

Robbins, who actually left Gentoo in 2004, certainly brings the benefit of
his experience in software management, which could support Microsoft's
Dynamic Systems Initiative (DSI) strategy for autonomic computing and
self?managing systems. Gentoo uses a software distribution system called
Portage that updates the distribution via a simple command and combined
"Portage tree" that is capable of creating and installing latest Gentoo
packages. Gentoo will also build customized packages to fit users'
specifications, optimized for hardware and requested features. Gentoo, which
describes itself as a "metadistribtion" for PCs, games and embedded systems,
runs on x86, AMD64, PowerPC, UltraSparc, Alpha and MIPS processors.

Interestingly, the recruitment of Robbins follows last year's release by
Microsoft of the WiX command line tools for installation of Windows from XML
packages to the community. WiX is now reportedly being used in MySQL 4.1 to
generate binaries for deployment of the open source database to Windows.
Microsoft also last year released its FlexWiki and Windows Template Library
(WTL) to SourceForge.?

option.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jun 15 11:44:55 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 15 Jun 2005 11:44:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Porting app from Windows to Linux
Message-ID: <20050615184455.GB5806@gate.kl-ic.com>


Greetings, Keith Lofstrom reporting live from the Design
Automation Conference in Anaheim CA.  These are the folks that
make software tools for chip design.  About 700 companies,
5000 exhibitor staff, 500K square feet.  Hundreds of companies
have Linux offerings.  There was one company showing off their
256-CPU + Hardware Acceleration "Optical Proximity Correction"
server (using FreeBSD).  IBM and Intel and AMD are showing off
their clusters - all Linux, of course .  A lot of the big CAD
tools require them.

One small company, run by a friend, has a personal IC layout tool.
The tool runs under Windoze right now, but he is strongly motivated
to port it to Linux.  It is mostly written in lemonade C, with
some chunks of acceleration code written directly in assembler.
The assembler code is simple data-structure in, data-structure out,
with no API calls.  He uses Trolltech QT for the GUI interface.

So, questions for the group:

1) What tools are available to help port the C and the assembler code?

2) Are there some good porting cookbooks out there?

This guy is ready to jump in.  How can we help make his experience
speedy and pleasurable?

Keith

Keith> 1) What tools are available to help port the C and the
Keith> assembler code?

Not sure about the assembler, but for C I tend to just throw gcc at it
and see where it chokes, fix, rinse, repeat.  Then check the output
to see if it generates the same thing as the original.  I'd be
inclined to ditch the assembler and recode that part in C.  Presumably
he understands what the assembler does.  Then profile and think about
whether the assembler still makes sense.

Russell Senior wrote:
|>>>>>"Keith" == Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> writes:
|
|
| Keith> 1) What tools are available to help port the C and the
| Keith> assembler code?
|
| Not sure about the assembler, but for C I tend to just throw gcc at it
| and see where it chokes, fix, rinse, repeat.  Then check the output
| to see if it generates the same thing as the original.  I'd be
| inclined to ditch the assembler and recode that part in C.  Presumably
| he understands what the assembler does.  Then profile and think about
| whether the assembler still makes sense.

Do you mean C or C++? If he's using Qt for the GUI already, it's likely
C++ (and if he's using Qt for most things, probably already pretty close
to ported). I suppose the best route to take is to find anywhere in the
code that uses Win32 calls directly and port those calls to the Qt
equivalent.  Another relevant portion is the toolchain he's currently
using: writing a decent Makefile or autotooling the project may be ...
difficult.

The assembler is likely in Intel syntax while almost all of the assembly
tools for Linux expect AT&T syntax.  They are largely the same with
minor annoying differences (like operand ordering).

A quick google brought up this reference which seems to be pretty good:
http://heather.cs.ucdavis.edu/~matloff/50/LinuxAssembly.html

There is also a tool to convert from Intel syntax to gas (GNU
Assembler/AT&T):
http://www.niksula.cs.hut.fi/~mtiihone/intel2gas/

However, his best alternative is going to be to use NASM, which works
under Windows and Linux (and a *lot* more platforms) and uses the Intel
syntax, I believe:
http://nasm.sourceforge.net/

- --
Shahms E. King <shahms at shahms.com>

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Wed Jun 15 18:44:27 2005
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Wed, 15 Jun 2005 18:44:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Porting app from Windows to Linux
In-Reply-To: <20050615184455.GB5806@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <200506160144.j5G1iT468453@pop.hevanet.com>

Keith,

Having worked 6 years on C compilers, you question is almost "non-sensical".

C is an easier way to write assembly.  I wrote in assembly for the first 10
years of my career.  With a good C compiler and some careful coding, you
should be able to get close to the "efficiency" of writing in assembly.

As for good compilers, Intel markets compilers (C and C++) that do *very*
good code generation and are GCC compatible.  This means porting using GCC
and tightening the screws with the Intel compiler.

Remember 90% of the execution time is typically spent in 10% (or less) of
the code.  Using profiling tools you should be able to isolate the 10%.  The
efficiency of the other 90% is of no consequence.

- Bill 

wamorita at hevanet.com

Yeah, the way the sources.list syntax works includes a URL and then a 
distribution name (unstable, here) and a list of repository names (main).

>> I don't remember the exact packages you need, but apt-cache should be 
>> able to help you there.

You want gmplayer and w32codecs.

You'll get the best video player and the codecs for playing all those 
proprietary formats.

The codecs ain't all Free, though.  So you taint yourself.

Glad to see it's all workin' for you, Mike.

The only way I knew to run the application was to right click on it and "open 
with"... so, no, I haven't run it from a terminal window. Your instructions 
would have been perfect, if I knew where my java binary was. I took a guess 
that ps ax would tell me and found that it did. I started the program the way 
I've done it in the past and then ran ps ax. It returned, among other things:

/usr/lib/SunJava2-1.4/jre/bin/java 
-jar /home/rsteff/ArtOfIllusion/ArtOfIllusion2.0/ArtOfIllusion.jar

So, with that in hand, I right clicked on the desktop, clicked on Create New, 
File, Link to Application. Then I filled in Art of Illusion as the name, and 
the above string as the command. Works just as expected.

Thanks for the hint. It was what I needed to get me thinking about how I could 
figure out where the Java binary lives, and it told me what else I'd need to 
know.

From russj at dimstar.net  Thu Jun 16 14:49:47 2005

For future reference many distributions come with "locate".  So the command

#locate java

Will return things with java in the name.

That's a lot of things with java in the name...

A little more selective...

#locate -r java$

does a regex search for things that end in java.

Much better.

If your system doesn't have locate (or rather slocate) then a find, 
piped to a grep will work. I use this on non-gnu systems...

#find / | grep java$

Just thought I'd pass that on, and maybe anticipate a question...

Russ


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Jun 16 14:51:03 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 16 Jun 2005 14:51:03 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: Resolved: [PLUG] How to start a Java program from the KDE desktop

On Thu, 16 Jun 2005, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Thanks for the hint. It was what I needed to get me thinking about how I
> could figure out where the Java binary lives, and it told me what else I'd
> need to know.

Dick,

   Two other useful tools: "which" and "locate."

   The former is used primarily with system executables and others; shows where they
are located. For example,

[rshepard at salmo ~]$ which java
/usr/lib/java/bin/java

   The latter is a synonym for "slocate" (secure locate) and, ultimately,
'whereis'. Each night the whereis database is updated with the location of
every file on your system. You can use it to find data files, images, or any
other type of file. For example,

[rshepard at salmo ~]$ locate .jar
/opt/opera-8.0-20050415.5-shared-qt.i386-en/java/opera.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/swriter.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/sbasic.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/sdraw.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/scalc.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/schart.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/smath.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/picture.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/simpress.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/help/en/common.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/program/classes/officebean.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/program/classes/juh.jar
/opt/OpenOffice.org1.1.4/program/classes/unoil.jar

   and a few dozen more.

HTH,

Rich

On Thu, Jun 16, 2005 at 02:15:46PM -0700, John Jordan wrote:
> 
> I'm pretty sure lbdvdread3 is installed. Also the other one, *css or
> something.

Have you run mplayer from a terminal window to see if it spits out any
useful information as it fails?

Note that in mainstream Debian, libdvdcss is not installed by any
official package. You have to run a script installed by the
libdvdread3 package, which in turn will go fetch libdvdcss from some
remote site. Read all about it:

   /usr/share/doc/libdvdread3/README.Debian

Looks like Ubuntu takes the same approach:

   https://wiki.ubuntu.com/RestrictedFormats

Reference section 8, "DVD-Video".


Paul

Quoth Rich Shepard, on Thu, 16 Jun 2005 14:51:03 -0700 (PDT):

> [rshepard at salmo ~]$ which java

"which" should have been the obvious solution here.  Sometimes the java
./bin/ directory doesn't get into the path as it should.  If "which
java" doesn't give you just what's needed, then you should find and add
that bin/ to your path.  It will probably avoid headaches later, when,
for example, you decide to try limewire.

--Jason

On Thu, 16 Jun 2005, John Jordan wrote:
> On 16 Jun 2005, at 13:22, AthlonRob wrote:
>> Aaron Baer wrote:
>>> I've always found ogle to be the most consistent application for 
>>> playing DVD's.
>>
>> Ditto.
>>
>> MPlayer for files, ogle for DVDs!  :-)
>
> Sell, so far I have tried Totem (installed by default on my Ubuntu 5.04 
> AMD-64), MPlayer, Ogle and VideoLan Client. None work.

We'll take a look at what works and in what ways.

> Totem pops up when I insert a movie DVD, and then announces it can't 
> play the DVD because of an "unspecified error."

OK, no idea.  Never used Totem.  Don't know anything about it.

> MPlayer starts to play the DVD, a screen flashes up for about one 
> second, and then the whole program disappears.

MPlayer doesn't do any DVD menu navigation, so what you're getting is the 
first track on the first title of the DVD, which is likely just the "FBI 
Warning" garbage.  It's far less than a second long and your machine is 
told to loop it some number of times when you're playing a video.

Try running MPlayer from the command line and throwing in a few different 
combinations
mplayer dvd://1
mplayer dvd://2

Just see what happens.

> Ogle and VLC do the same, except they don't even flash a screen up for a 
> second. They just go away.

OK, so what exactly are you doing with ogle?  For me, I run ogle and it 
gives me the little floating toolbar and I choose "open disc" from the 
menu.  As soon as that happens, I get the DVD menu (sometimes with an "FBI 
Warning" beforehand).  Are you choosing "Open disc" and getting some other 
behavior?  Can you describe it in any detail?

> I'm pretty sure lbdvdread3 is installed. Also the other one, *css or 
> something.

Well, Debian, being based in the USA, doesn't distribute all of the decss 
code with their system, but it's available from other sources if you are 
one of those people who can run it legally.  You have to run some commands 
by hand in order to make it work properly.  A list of those commands (in a 
format that you or your machine can understand) is part of the libdvdread3 
package and is here:

/usr/share/doc/libdvdread3/examples/install-css.sh

So, you know, if you are able to run that thing, you can make sure you 
have up-to-date dvdcss libraries.

> I can browse the files on the DVD, so reading the media is not the 
> problem. All I can think of is that it's some security issue.

Cartel security, yes.  The discs are designed to work only with players 
that are manufactured by licensees.  It's just a little scheme for picking 
up money on both ends of the transaction and keeping competition at a 
minimum.  It's all a very natural part of industrial capitalism.  Hell, 
it's practically the reason we have government.

J.

option at bootup is to run it live. I did so, and it came up OK. Then I 
wandered around in the files trying to see if there was anything 
obvious. There was xorg.conf, and an xorg.conf.200506170447 file. 
The end of that file is the timestamp when I screwed things up. 
Evidently the reconfigure utility made a backup of xorg.conf. So I 
made a copy of xorg.conf as -bak and then did a copy of 
xorg.conf.200506170447 to xorg.conf. Then I rebooted.

X still won't come up.

There are no system backups because I haven't got that far in 
learning Linx. Anyway, I have no personal data on this computer, 
so there is nothing critical to back up yet.

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Thu Jun 16 22:41:20 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 16 Jun 2005 22:41:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Killed Ubuntu X
In-Reply-To: <42B1FBA6.29466.589AD878@localhost>

Message-ID: <200506162241.20210.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# John Jordan
# on Thursday 16 June 2005 10:22 pm:

>I got to the third line in the instructions. 

you mean:
  sudo dpkg-reconfigure xserver-xorg
?

>This brought up a non-GUI 
>series of settings.

I'm guessing that's xf86config

>I got almost all the way through (to the point 
>where it asked what default color depth to set) and it dumped me
>back to the command line. I repeated it about a dozen times. No
>matter what I entered, I always got dumped back to the command
>line at the same point.

That's because dpkg-reconfigure is just calling a set of commands 
associated with the xserver-xorg package (I guess.  Though I don't 
offhand know how to find out what those commands are.)

So.  No matter how many times your run xf86config, it won't dump you 
back into X.

Go back through and give it all of the options that you gave it the 
first time.  Then do 'startx' (or maybe '/etc/init.d/xdm start' if you 
usually start from a display manager.)

Hint:  startx will show (EE) on your screen when there's something wrong 
with your config file.  Check /var/log/X<mumble> for the full story.

--Eric

   $ dpkg -l | grep mplayer
   ii  mplayer-doc    1.0-pre7-0.0   Documentation for mplayer
   ii  mplayer-fonts  3.5-2          Fonts for mplayer

Ubuntu install, but have no experience to back that notion up.

just fine, while later versions don't play at all. Seems like a lot of

Windows-land videos with Xine (at least on PowerPC).

Date: Thu, 16 Jun 2005 15:06:15 -0700
From: Allen C Brown <allen_brown at agilent.com>
To: Corvallis Linux User's Group <lug at peak.org>,
     Eugene Unix and Gnu/Linux User Group <euglug at euglug.org>
Subject: [lug] Open source opportunity

I have stumbled across an opportunity to move my employer
more in the direction of open source.  The need:

* compiler for Coldfire processors
* support for above

I know that GCC provides Coldfire compilation.  But Agilent
is unlikely to go this route without knowing they can get
support.  Any suggestions?

Support is likely to consist of expert advice.  It may include
creation of macros to extend support to instructions which are
too new for GCC.  Or in the extreme case, extending GCC.

What I am looking for is recommendations for who can provide
support.  Commercial support is acceptable.  Even active forums
that provide support may be adequate.  The customer is mostly
in Boise.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Jun 17 12:52:27 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 17 Jun 2005 12:52:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forbes: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea18050617122954d6292c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <ff01ea18050617122954d6292c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <42B329FB.8010201@madboa.com>

On 6/17/05 12:29 PM, Steve D... wrote:

> Is Linux For Losers? 
> http://www.forbes.com/business/businesstech/2005/06/16/linux-bsd-unix-cz_dl_0616theo.html
> 
> Theo's a smart guy.  You'd think he'd have enough sense to tone it 
> down a bit for the general media.  It doesn't look good for either
> the BSD or Linux communities.

I've had *BSD guys give coherent, reasonable explanations why they 
dislike the Linux codebase -- although I think they typically base their 
judgements on stuff they saw back in 2.0 and 2.2 days. They can rightly 
point to some driver code that looks as if it were thrown together by 
amateur programmers during a late-night Jolt session.

The thing is, reasonable folks are usually able to articulate the 
trade-offs: a focus on broad driver support, rapid development, and 
world domination (Linux) vs. stability, audited code, and a much greater 
willingness to be a niche player (*BSD).

I don't think Theo's assessments are completely wrong, but they don't 
give anything resembling a clear picture of what's at stake in the 
choice between the operating systems.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com


From jason at vancleve.com  Fri Jun 17 13:00:39 2005
From: jason at vancleve.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Fri, 17 Jun 2005 13:00:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forbs: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea18050617122954d6292c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <ff01ea18050617122954d6292c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20050617130039.5972f27d.jason@vancleve.com>

Quoth Steve D..., on Fri, 17 Jun 2005 12:29:36 -0700:

> Is Linux For Losers? 

No, linux is for people who are willing to sacrifice the convenience of
mainstream, point-and-click software interfaces for personal freedom
and, ultimately, freedom of innovation.  Yes, linux includes a great
deal of crappy software:  it is inclusive by nature.  But I think for
Raadt to rail against it like that may be a cheap way to try to induce
linux developers to shape up, or he's simply missing the point, or he's
just bitter.

He complains about a single comment in the kernel source, like "does
this belong here?", concluding that it shows the whole thing is a
ticking time bomb of instability.  This also implies that BSD's code is
absolutely immaculate.  Get real, man.  I'd rather my software's
imperfections were documented than swept under the rug.

The linux kernel beats windows, but much of what runs on it sucks
indeed, notwithstanding the fact that most of it was developed as a
public service.  I wish it weren't so, but nonetheless, I'll continue to
advocate linux, in a realistic fashion (to those who can handle it),
until Microsoft is finally cut down to size and open standards prevail.

--Jason V. C.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri Jun 17 14:17:21 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 17 Jun 2005 14:17:21 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Presentations Created With LaTeX
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506171412180.29740@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I know that there are other LaTeX users here who need to make oral presentations
using computer generated "slides." LaTeX has had the prosper and seminar
classes available for a while, but there's one that is much better: beamer.

   The beamer class produces pdf output but allows the full typesetting power
of LaTeX to be used. It's really a nice tool; I just finished my first
presentation with it and it does produce impressive, OS-agnostic results.
I'll have a copy of the pdf on my web site after I create some graphics for
it and get those inserted.

   You can find the beamer.cls and beamer.sty on CTAN. It works _very_ well
with LyX.

From rich at paranoid.org  Fri Jun 17 14:33:38 2005
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Fri, 17 Jun 2005 14:33:38 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Forbs: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <20050617201947.40417.qmail@web50603.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <ff01ea18050617122954d6292c@mail.gmail.com>
	<20050617201947.40417.qmail@web50603.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <19206.70.57.126.130.1119044018.squirrel@o4.hostbaby.com>

On Fri, June 17, 2005 1:19 pm, Robert Kopp said:
>
> I use FreeBSD (which I suppose is similar to OpenBSD)
> once in a while, and it pretty much rocks. It doesn't
> have as many applications as Linux, though.

OpenBSD is similar. It split off from the NetBSD project years ago.

One of the big things that sets the OpenBSD project in its own niche is
their focus on security. They did audits of the old BSD code that many of
the *BSD projects shared, and have made a real point of providing lots of
good security tools.

FreeBSD is probably a lot friendlier to the average end user. It's one of
my favorite Unixes, and works great for web serving. OpenBSD is great too
in its own way, it's the box of choice for making a Unix firewall with OSS
tools, IMO.

Theo is known for being "difficult," as Linus put it, but he is not the
first person to question whether it's just far too easy to get sloppy code
into the kernel, and what impact that has on stability. Working as a
smaller group of programmers has advantages and disadvantages. They
probably do produce better code than some of the Linux developers, but
you're not as likely to get a driver right away for a new device on the
*BSDs.

When it comes to stability, there are people who would prefer to spec out
a box that they know is supported by their favorite BSD and run that,
rather than let their choice of OS be driven by the availability of
drivers. There are also less apps available for openBSD, but most of the
major OSS packages are ported for it.


Rich

I haven't looked at T-bird, yet, but since it shares a heritage with Netscape 
Communicator, there's the possibility that feature is the same. If you 
haven't already done so, look along the left edge of the window. Is there a 
"button" cleverly hidden in the border? In Netscape, it looks like a vertical 
line about 1/2" tall with arrowheads pointing towards the message pane when 
the list of folders is hidden, and towards the left edge of the window when 
they are displayed. It works just like the "button" that displays and hides 
the message pane in the line that separates the message pane from the list of 
messages. 

I don't find a menu entry to hide/display the folder list in Netscape, either.
