In the interest of keeping Windows XP happy, I installed it 
to the first partition on the hard drive and the partition 
was rather large.  I used a Linux system on the rest of the 
drive to back up the bits.  XP is FAT32 and I use a
Windows 98 boot disk to go via loadlin into Linux.
This avoids the problem on two other XP systems I've set 
up where the primary drive is F something, but it makes 
it a real hassle to get to Linux and it exposes XP's
filesystem.

What I want to do is make a bootable freedos cd with loadlin 
and the kernel on it instead.  I have found that a copy of 
dos from, http://www.freedos.org/, works well with loadlin.
This would be a good replacement for a 98 boot disk.
With this cd, the XP partition can be updated to NTFS
as it won't need to be accessible anymore to boot Linux.

Grub installed to a bootable cdrom might work also.  

I want to stay away from any Linux bootloader on the beginning 
of the drive to avoid a, giving it away that Linux or anything 
else is installed and b, problems associated with the use of 
dd to put the bits back.  I also want to avoid some Windows 
event removing this bootloader.  The only irregularity I'm not
avoiding because I don't want to use two hard disks is
giving Windows XP less than 100% of the drive.  I'm thinking a
small 50 meg fat partition hidden by XOSL at the start of the
drive might work, but I don't want to go that route for
cases where it shouldn't be widely known that another OS 
is installed.

I wonder if the guest program for my zip drive works in FreeDos?
I might be able to sys a zip disk under Freedos and somehow burn
an image to cd of that zip disk that will boot.

From russj at dimstar.net  Sat Jun 18 03:42:45 2005
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 03:42:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Booting Linux from CD...
In-Reply-To: <1118966489.42b212d920fd2@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1118966489.42b212d920fd2@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <42B3FAA5.7000801@dimstar.net>

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:

>What I want to do is make a bootable freedos cd with loadlin 
>and the kernel on it instead.  I have found that a copy of 
>dos from, http://www.freedos.org/, works well with loadlin.
>This would be a good replacement for a 98 boot disk.
>With this cd, the XP partition can be updated to NTFS
>as it won't need to be accessible anymore to boot Linux.
>  
>
Why not just do what I've been doing for 10 years.

1> Install Windows as normal, with a 20 gig or so partition.
2> Install Linux in the 2nd and 3rd partition.
3> Put the Linux bootloader of choice (lilo or grub) in the boot sector 
of the root linux partition. Leave the Windows boot loader in the MBR 
where it wants to be.
4> Make the root linux partition active, if it isn't already.

Lilo or grub will boot the system, and if you choose Windows, the 
Windows boot loader takes over, and it doesn't know the difference.

Some questions are better left unasked.

As for the boot sector getting blown away by some event, that's why you
want a good rescue CD. Then you can boot off of it and re-install your
boot loader. Some distros provide rescue CDs (I know the Gentoo universal
install CD is live and works for this), and there are other good ones you
can download. Knoppix works great, and I like this rescue CD a lot:

http://www.sysresccd.org/


Rich


From rich at paranoid.org  Sat Jun 18 08:53:48 2005
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 08:53:48 -0700 (PDT)

On Sat, June 18, 2005 7:06 am, Rich Shepard said:
>    The Register has a very interesting article on Theo's Ballmeresque
> bashing
> of linux. Makes sense to me.
>
>       <http://www.theregister.com/2005/06/17/de_raadt_channels_ballmer/>

I agree with it to a certain extent, but I don't agree with putting all of
the emphasis on the vendors. Not everyone who upgrades a Linux kernel does
it because their vendor has released a new version of their OS. Some
people do it on their own because there is a driver they need, a bug that
has been fixed, or some other new feature.

Theo's comments are pretty extreme, but I have heard some of those same
concerns expressed by people in the Linux community. It's just a very
different style of development when you have a small core group of people
who are writing your kernel code. There are tradeoffs on both sides. If I
was looking for a rock-solid Open Source OS for a web server, I might very
well go with one of the BSDs (probably FreeBSD, but maybe OpenBSD if
security was a major concern). If I was building a desktop box, I would be
more likely to go with Linux so I could get whatever goofy new driver I
wanted.


Rich

From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Jun 18 10:08:53 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 10:08:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Envy: Another Perspective
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506180704461.11019@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, Jun 18, 2005 at 07:06:06AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   The Register has a very interesting article on Theo's Ballmeresque bashing
> of linux. Makes sense to me.
>      <http://www.theregister.com/2005/06/17/de_raadt_channels_ballmer/>

Theo's not wrong, but he does miss the point.  Everyone's using Linux
because it works with most anything, it's easy to install and it's
widely available in a variety of forms.  Linux's loose and open
development model gives someone who wants to hack on it a serious
chance of payoff, i.e.  getting a patch accepted.

The licensing makes a difference, too.  Linux is GPL, so private
companies' improvements go into the public codebase and get built on,
while the BSD license allows companies to keep improvements private.

Combine that with the BSDs' professional* management, and you get much
slower growth than Linux.  Slow growth is good IMHO, but it doesn't
sell consluting and hardware, which is what HP and IBM want to do.


* XFree86 has suffered from "professional" management for quite a few
years.  Hence the XOrg fork.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jun 18 10:46:49 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 10:46:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forbes: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <42B3B709.5080708@schluting.com>
References: <20050617201947.40417.qmail@web50603.mail.yahoo.com>
	<42B3B709.5080708@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <20050618174648.GA9831@gate.kl-ic.com>


The disadvantage of Linux is that just about anybody can contribute
to the kernel and infrastructure.

The advantage of Linux is that just about anybody can contribute
to the kernel and infrastructure.

Whether the Linux style is advantageous or not depends on what you need
to get done.  As it is, there are a number of things that ought to be 
Linux kernel modules that aren't there yet (like hot-swap IDE in 2.6.X),
but I doubt they will ever get there under the narrow-focus "professional"
management of the BSD families.  Feature richness isn't BSD's goal. 
Although the Linux bashing by some BSD types is just arrogant bigotry,
they are correct that many tasks are better suited to BSD, like
single-function servers.  If the BSD folk would highlight the areas
they excel in, rather than just insult Linux developers, or petulantly
whine that BSD is "better for everything" in some ill-defined way, I
would be a lot more likely to pay attention to them. 

I used flavors of BSD from 1980 through 2000, and changed over to Linux
because I like the attitude of that community better.  I am glad that
there are people in both communities that value cooperation and
interoperability over status games, so I can easily migrate back
and forth as my needs change.

If you want stability and maximum security, go for BSD.  If you want
feature richness, go for Linux.  If you want diversity, be glad there
are both.  Let's not leave a portion of the open-source map uncovered.

Keith

increasingly common for people to use false arguments for/against their 
favorite OS, and it gets quite annoying :)

From llywrch at agora.rdrop.com  Sat Jun 18 12:10:00 2005
From: llywrch at agora.rdrop.com (Geoff Burling)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 12:10:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Forbes: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <42B329FB.8010201@madboa.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0506181149400.567-100000@joan.burling.com>

On Fri, 17 Jun 2005, Paul Heinlein wrote:

> On 6/17/05 12:29 PM, Steve D... wrote:
>
> > Is Linux For Losers?
> > http://www.forbes.com/business/businesstech/2005/06/16/linux-bsd-unix-cz_dl_0616theo.html
> >
> > Theo's a smart guy.  You'd think he'd have enough sense to tone it
> > down a bit for the general media.  It doesn't look good for either
> > the BSD or Linux communities.
>
> I've had *BSD guys give coherent, reasonable explanations why they
> dislike the Linux codebase -- although I think they typically base their
> judgements on stuff they saw back in 2.0 and 2.2 days. They can rightly
> point to some driver code that looks as if it were thrown together by
> amateur programmers during a late-night Jolt session.
>
> The thing is, reasonable folks are usually able to articulate the
> trade-offs: a focus on broad driver support, rapid development, and
> world domination (Linux) vs. stability, audited code, and a much greater
> willingness to be a niche player (*BSD).
>
Sounds like the old Best is better vs. Worse is better arguments that have
been floating around the computer industry for at least a couple of
decades.

The dispute can be summarized as follows:
* Best is better -- you have a group of programmers who write perfect
code that gets relased within a reasonable time frame. The code is
elegant, matches the spec precisely & works with a minimum of flaws.
* Worse is better -- you have a group of programmers who write code
that does most of what you want, & is released fairly rapidly. The
code may be ugly, doesn't match the spec (if one exists), & often has
major problems -- however, using feedback from users (e.g., comments,
proferred code), the code improves.

(I've probably mangled these 2 positions; I'm writing all of this from
memory. Feel free to Google on these phrases & correct what I've written.)

Each POV has its strengths & weaknesses. "Best is better" is defended
because -- well, the code works like it's supposed to: it's solid,
it was written following best practices, & it was written by experts.
"Worse is better" is defended because of speed of deployment (amongst
other points), & the fact that with user input or feedback (analogies
here include the metaphor of the marketplace, & genetic algorthyms)
the code gradually improves in the parts where the users need it to
improve.

Also, the "Best is better" model requires a way to pay its programmers,
who for some reason need a paycheck & benefits; the "Worse is better"
model can be carried to a large degree by volunteers & part-time
amateurs who don't need a paycheck -- although IIRC they don't refuse
the offer of money. Unfortunately, volunteers can be a flakey lot, &
that is why there are a lot of dead projects on Freshmeat & other
repositories of free software: they died due to lack of interest, the
maintainer got bored/went into another line of work/died.

The argument over these 2 approaches will go on until the end of
sentient life because both produce useful software -- just in different
ways. In other words, it's a religious war: you either embrace one
dogma & label everyone who disagrees with you a heretic (or worse),
or be an agnostic, & use what works.



From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sat Jun 18 13:56:20 2005
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 13:56:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Forbes: Is Linux For Losers?
In-Reply-To: <42B46F4C.9090800@schluting.com>
References: <20050617201947.40417.qmail@web50603.mail.yahoo.com>	<42B3B709.5080708@schluting.com>	<20050618174648.GA9831@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<42B46F4C.9090800@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <42B48A74.1000906@cesmail.net>



Charlie Schluting wrote:

>
> I really don't understand what you mean about single-function servers.
> IMHO the memory management and scheduling in FreeBSD is far superior
> to Linux's. Also, it's pretty common for linux users to install
> FreeBSD (with the same WM) and notice a signifigantly better desktop
> experience.

I haven't looked at FreeBSD (or any other BSD) memory management and
scheduler, but I have spent a fair amount of time with those in Linux.
"Superior" is of course a value judgement, and I will say that memory
management and scheduler design is a difficult task. Still, the way it's
done in Linux -- a combination of code, algorithms, heuristics, patches
to code, hard-coded parameters and tuneables -- is the worst of all
possible worlds as far as I'm concerned.

There's an awful lot of computer science theory and non-trivial
mathematics involved. I've been studying it for many years and I can't
claim to know it all. But it's absolutely inconceivable to me that a
"commercial enterprise OS product", the Red Hat Enterprise Linux 3
kernel, has *any* hard-coded scheduler tuning parameters! Yet it does --
the source RPM is in the public domain and you can look for yourself.

So what you have in RHEL -- and probably any other Linux -- is a kernel
pre-tuned to the test cases, both real-world and synthetic benchmarks.
It is *not* tunable by the system adminstrator to the enterprise's
workload, unless that system administrator is willing to do a lot of
kernel recompiles, reboots and experiments on live machines.

The good news is that our neighbors at OSDL and IBM are working on this,
and, since Linux is an open source effort, it will get fixed and anyone
(including myself :) ) can get involved. Meanwhile, what's there is
"good enough for most purposes", I guess.

not approved by your distribution of choice.

it in their distribution in their "stable" release without checking it
for stability, then it is the vendor's fault. With a mere 6 month release
cycle, you think Fedora is going through very much testing? Do you think
each release provides a lot of new features? No and no.

> Theo's comments are pretty extreme, but I have heard some of those same

No, if you're going for a rock-solid server, you use something setup for

certainly do not go for Fedora or one of the other bleeding edge
distributions.

are much better known. While few people mention Debian, RedHat Enterprise
or SuSE Enterprise. They might be less sexy, but those are no less
important than the former.

points is not the one that is offered by right clicking on a .jar file.

# echo $PATH
...:/usr/lib/java/jre/bin:...

which and locate are good things to know about, but they wouldn't have told me 
as much as ps ax did in this case. With ps ax, I not only saw which binary 
was running, but also the command line option to use to load the .jar file.

/usr/lib/SunJava2-1.4/jre/bin/java 
-jar /home/rsteff/ArtOfIllusion/ArtOfIllusion2.0/ArtOfIllusion.jar

On the other hand, which did show me that there's more than one java on my 
machine. I suppose I should try the one that which showed me some day and see 
if there are any differences that I can notice.

Thanks to all.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

Note Cards & Photographic Prints avaliable at:
http://home.comcast.net/~rcsphoto/

>From: Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com>
> On Sat, 18 Jun 2005, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> > Yet, there is a Linux qualifier too, Debian. You most certainly do not go
> > for Fedora or one of the other bleeding edge distributions.
> >
> > Perhaps the problem is that super bleeding edge distributions like Fedora
> > are much better known. While few people mention Debian, RedHat Enterprise
> > or SuSE Enterprise. They might be less sexy, but those are no less
> > important than the former.
> 
>    What proves your point is Slackware ("the quiet distribution"). Now in its
> eleventh year, Slackware is one of the old timers still in existence, and it
> is far back from the bleeding edge. Patrick includes a 2.6.x kernel for those
> who want it, but the 2.4.x is the default. I've had some issues with
> applications after an upgrade, but not with the core utilities. It's been a
> very pleasant change from my Red Hat days (which ended with 7.3). Heck, other
> than security replacements, I do no distribution package upgrades from one
> release to the next.

Though modern Slackware may also qualify (been too long for me to say
whether or not it meets the qualification), Debian stable easily
qualifies as well. Each of those things you mentioned is true of Debian
stable as well. It wasn't until the release last week that Debian stable
even had support for the 2.6 kernel.

Anyway, not time for a distro war here. The point is that the second
article was very true. Theo's was comparing *BSD to absolute bleeding
edge distributions, rather than the stable distributions that are a far
better comparison for the characteristics he was looking at.

Not to be pedantic, here, but you made a certain mistake four times in a
row, so you may not be aware of it:  the word "too", not "to", is used
to denote excessiveness.  Hence,

> only do what I pay for it to do - the limits are TOO
> restrictive. It is TOO hard to use - no multiple windows - slows 
> down when TOO many programs run with it, spends TOO much time 
> fixing itself.

. . . That's just in case you care to improve your English (which makes
it more respectable and less cumbersome to read).  No offense.

--Jason Van Cleve

it because my IP "does not have a reverse DNS entry".

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Jun 20 17:29:30 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 17:29:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] IT'S WORKING!!!
Message-ID: <42B6FCFA.2246.6C280B09@localhost>

Thanks to Derek at the Clinic and a kind person on the Linux 
R3000 e-list that I subscribe to, my wireless is NOW WORKING!!

At the clinic we got close, but were still getting a stack of 
"ndiswrapper complains about" messages when we did a dmesg. 
At the conclusion of the Clinic we were under the impression that I 
might have to do some compiling. Yeah, me, someone who has 
been using Linux for a month and a half is going to compile 
something in Linux!

But after going back and forth on the R3000 e-list it turns out that 
what was wrong was that I was using the wrong Linux-image for the 
ndiswrapper that I had. Once I switched to the right Linux-image, 
the wireless came right up! I'm so excited.

Classes started today at Portland State so I had to go there 
anyway. I took the computer and (with a few minutes help from IT 
Dept) actually got into Pub-Net via wireless. I still have to figure out 
other stuff -- but I've never used wifi even on a Windows computer, 
so it will take me a while.

Exciting stuff!!

General News

***Historical Maps Online 
David Rumsey writes about his collection of more than 150,000 historical
maps of the Americas and the world, many of which he has made available
free to the public in an online map library. At O'Reilly's Where 2.0
Conference, David will draw on his personal map collection, as well as his
work with geographic information systems, to discuss how information of
all kinds has been mapped and will be mapped in the future.
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/06/02/davidrumsey.html


***Creating a Textbook My Way: An Interview with Charles Anderson
Charles Anderson is an assistant professor in the Division of Computer
Science at Western Oregon University, teaching mostly networking and
operating system courses. Being very particular about the materials
covered in his classes, he's had difficulty finding appropriate and timely
textbooks. Learn how Charles used SafariU to create his custom textbook,
while avoiding copyright fair use limitations and the time-consuming
process of gathering appropriate materials. Check out the SafariU homepage

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Jun 20 17:44:11 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 17:44:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?
Message-ID: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>

OK, at this point I have quite a bit of effort invested in configurations 
on my Ubuntu-64 Compaq. I feel it's time to start making backups.

The backup medium will be an external USB drive connected to my 
Windows 2000 computer. I created a share on the Windows 2000 
computer and the Linux laptop sees the drive just fine. (Except it 
asks for my password for the Windows computer each time I try to 
access it the first time during a session, which may be a stumbling 
block for unattended backups.)

I googled for "linux backup" and got all kinds of stuff. Almost all 
was commercial software and designed for massive installations. 
The rest seemed improper for one reason or another.

I could backup from my Windows 2000 computer, except I haven't 
been able to get the Windows 2000 computer to see the drive on 
the laptop. It sees the laptop, and when I click to browse it, 
Windows pops up a login box. Un fortunately, when I enter my 
username and password for the laptop, Windows keeps saying it is 
wrong. I've tried a few things, like putting //laptopname/ in front of 
my username, but nothing works. Anyway, it just seems more 
logical to back up each computer from that computer. I'll deal with 
the Windows network issue later.

So does anyone have any suggestions for simple backup solutions 
that would work in my situation, and without requiring a degree in 
CompSci to run it?


From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Mon Jun 20 20:08:23 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 20:08:23 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?
In-Reply-To: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>
References: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0506201937300.4698@localhost>

Why not hang the USB drive on the linux laptop directly, instead of
using the Windows 2000 machine as nothing more than a USB host?

Barring that, you probably just need some samba config tweaks to get
your Windows 2000 load to be capable of mounting the samba share.

I'm no samba expert (I don't own a Windows box and I have no reason to
ever access one in my daily life), but I do have samba configured
adequately to do what you want, so here's how I did it.  Here's my
smb.conf file:

[global]
     netbios name = jungle
     netbios aliases = jungle
     server string = Samba Server on jungle
     workgroup = WORKGROUP
     encrypt passwords = true
     map to guest = Bad User
     null passwords = Yes
     username map = /etc/samba/smbusers
     password level = 8
     username level = 5
     log level = 2
     max log size = 0
     announce as = NT Workstation
     socket options = TCP_NODELAY SO_RCVBUF=8192 SO_SNDBUF=8192
     os level = 99
     lm announce = No
     preferred master = No
     enhanced browsing = Yes
     dns proxy = No
     wins support = Yes
     valid users = nobody,root,ckonstan
     create mask = 0777
     directory mask = 0777
     guest ok = Yes
     hosts allow = 127.0.0.1 192.168.0. 192.168.2. 192.168.8.
     hosts deny = 0.0.0.0

[homes]
     comment = Home Directories
     valid users = %S
     read only = No
     create mask = 0755
     force create mode = 0755
     security mask = 0755
     directory mask = 0755
     directory security mask = 0755
     inherit permissions = Yes
     guest ok = No

[printers]
     comment = All Printers
     path = /var/spool/samba
     printable = Yes
     postscript = Yes
     browseable = No

[public]
     comment = Public share on jungle
     path = /var/samba/public
     read only = No
     create mask = 0777
     force create mode = 0777
     security mask = 0777
     directory mask = 0777
     directory security mask = 0777
     inherit permissions = Yes
     guest ok = Yes

In the [global] section, the important line is:
     encrypt passwords = true
And probably also:
     null passwords = Yes

It's been a really long time since I had to figure this stuff out, so
forgive me if I forget the details.  It seems to me that if you say
"encrypt passwords = true", you need one or both of the following files in
/etc/samba/:

     smbpasswd
     smbusers

You add and modify entries in smbpasswd with the "smbpasswd" command.
"smbpasswd -a <username> adds a new samba user.  smbusers is written by
hand.  Its format is "<username> = <alias1>[, <alias2>, <alias3>]".  One
entry per line works.

Since the password encryption is NT-style (again, I may be wrong, shoot me, but
Windows is happy with it), you can now mount those pesky shares (listed in
the [public] and [homes] sections of the example smb.conf file).  But
you are going to be prompted for a password, even if the password is
blank.  Not automation-friendly.

We have a samba server on our network at my place of work.  It is a
linux box, with samba set up very much like here (except it is also the
master browser).  The Windows clients have no trouble with it.  It was
my unhappy task for many months to administer this service.  If I had to
set up a network-based backup plan, I would steer well clear of samba.

Install cygwin on the Windows box, and use rsync from a cron job running
in cygwin.  As long as the Windows machine is acting as the client in an
rsync/ssh connection, it'll work fine.  rsync is a vastly superior choice
for backups, since it is all about creating a backup mirror image with
the least number of transfered bytes possible.  That samba-based backup
script will do a blanket re-copy of all files, which isn't efficient.

Carlos

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Mon Jun 20 22:32:36 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 22:32:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# John Jordan
# on Monday 20 June 2005 10:05 pm:

>Did I miss where the free simple backup utility is located?

Just fire-up a shell and do this:

  tar -czvf mybackup.tar.gz /etc/

That's about as simple as it gets.  If you want it graphically, there's 
ark.

Now you have a backup, I suppose you would like to schedule it :-)  A 
lot of people do this with cron, but that oftens ends-up a bit ugly.

I've been working on (ok, haven't been working on) what I call the "bag 
of hammers backup" program.  I call it that because it's dumber than a 
bag of hammers to backup a linux system onto a windows share (not that 
it's dumb of you to do it, rather that it's the smartest thing you can 
do if you have to talk to something as stupid as a windows box.)  When 
you're talking to something smarter, you can use programs like dirvish, 
rbackup, etc.

In any case, the idea was that you could set it up even if you were 
dumber than a bag of hammers.  I'm still working on that user-friendly 
bit though.  It's essentially just a fancy scheduler/configurator for 
the above tar command (but does rolling backups, etc.)

The code is in my svn/, though it hasn't been finished to the point that 
I thought it deserved packaging.  In particular, there's some stuff 
that happens in the guts of Getopt::Helpful that I've been trying to 
work-out which bohbackup needs to work right.

I haven't found much else that really does the job in the situation 
where a linux box is among a windows network and not a server.  Kind of 
rare I guess except when you're a newbie or you're just getting a shop 
started on Linux.

--Eric

From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Jun 20 22:39:44 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 22:39:44 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?
In-Reply-To: <20050621051528.0CCFE42E7@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200506210539.j5L5di92014589@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> Did I miss where the free simple backup utility is located?

There are three utilities generally used for this purpose. `rsync`, this
is used for simply duplicating the filesystem tree, often over a network,
but can also be used locally. `tar`, "tape archive", reasonably effective
as a backup tool. `dump/restore`, a pair of tools specifically designed
for backups, dump is filesystem specific and brutal in its approach, but
wonderful for its intended purpose, mainly backups.

Often the output archive from dump or tar is run through `gzip` or
`bzip2`. Both are compression programs, plain and simple. gzip is the
older, while bzip2 compresses better but is slower. Notably none of the
above tools do compression, that is a seperate step from archiving.

Putting it together, for dump:

dump -u#f - <FS> | gzip > archive-<FS>.#.gz

Where # would be the level of the dump (0 is full, 1-9 are layers of
incrementals). <FS> is the filesystem, each has to be done seperately,
so "/", "/usr", "/var", and "/home" each needs to be done depending on
your installation.

For restoring:

gunzip -c archive-<FS>.#.gz | restore -rf -

Starting with the level 0, and going through each incremental in turn.
After you've done all the levels, you delete the file "restoresymtab".


And now it is time for the holy war on tar versus dump to get going
again.  :-/

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Jun 20 22:46:56 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 22:46:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?
In-Reply-To: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>
References: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>
Message-ID: <20050621054656.GA28451@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Jun 20, 2005 at 05:44:11PM -0700, John Jordan wrote:
> OK, at this point I have quite a bit of effort invested in configurations 
> on my Ubuntu-64 Compaq. I feel it's time to start making backups.
> 
> The backup medium will be an external USB drive connected to my 
> Windows 2000 computer. I created a share on the Windows 2000 
> computer and the Linux laptop sees the drive just fine. (Except it 
> asks for my password for the Windows computer each time I try to 
> access it the first time during a session, which may be a stumbling 
> block for unattended backups.)
> 
> I googled for "linux backup" and got all kinds of stuff. Almost all 
> was commercial software and designed for massive installations. 
> The rest seemed improper for one reason or another.
> 
> I could backup from my Windows 2000 computer, except I haven't 
> been able to get the Windows 2000 computer to see the drive on 
> the laptop. It sees the laptop, and when I click to browse it, 
> Windows pops up a login box. Un fortunately, when I enter my 
> username and password for the laptop, Windows keeps saying it is 
> wrong. I've tried a few things, like putting //laptopname/ in front of 
> my username, but nothing works. Anyway, it just seems more 
> logical to back up each computer from that computer. I'll deal with 
> the Windows network issue later.
> 
> So does anyone have any suggestions for simple backup solutions 
> that would work in my situation, and without requiring a degree in 
> CompSci to run it?

Are you concerned about the security of your backup machine?  Once
the backup machine is compromised, everything you back up on it
is compromised also.  You might think about a Linux-based backup
server instead - or at least a Windoze machine with no inbound 
services, one that is never used for browsing or email (to name
two common vulnerabilities).

If you go to a Linux server, consider dirvish (www.dirvish.org),
an open source project I am managing.  We have a small but active
community, about 60 on the mailing list.  dirvish has some problems
backing up windoze machines, but we do have users doing that.  Get
on the mailing list and ask how folks are doing...

The main advantage of dirvish is that it uses rsync, which is very
fast over the network, and uses Linux hardlinks on the target,
which means that it can overlay many daily images over the same
set of files.  I have 300 days worth of 100GB backups on one 250GB
disk;  these take about 1 hour per night to back up 6 systems over
the ethernet.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

On 20 Jun 2005, at 22:46, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> Are you concerned about the security of your backup machine?  Once the
> backup machine is compromised, everything you back up on it is
> compromised also.  You might think about a Linux-based backup server
> instead - or at least a Windoze machine with no inbound services, one
> that is never used for browsing or email (to name two common
> vulnerabilities).

I am concerned very little with the security of the backups. 
Remember, I am not backing up an organization with thousands of 
users and gigabytes of sensitive data. I am just protecting my own 
stuff from a hard disk failure. No one would be remotely interested 
in hacking into my home network. There is nothing here to interest 
them. Unless they wanted my latest term paper, that is. :)

And, as for the Windows 2000 computer, it is behind a router and 
has a firewall (Sygate). Looking at Sygate's logs, in the past 
couple of years since installing Sygate, nothing has gotten past it. 
I am absolutely positive that there are people, even on this e-list, 
who would be capable of getting into my computer. But life is a 
series of risk calculations. This is one I am not concerned about.

The Windows computer does not have anything from Microsoft 
installed other than Windows 2000. Even then, Internet Explorer is 
blocked from the internet on the Sygate firewall. Outlook and Office 
are not installed. I uses Pegasus for mail, and I definitely know 
better than to click on an attachment. I have been using this 
Windows 2000 computer connected via Comcast cable for the past 
3.5 years. It has never had a virus, worm, or any other malware.

So basically, I am not concerned about security in keeping the 
backups on an eternal USB disk connected to the Windows 2000 
computer. I think I am in much greater risk of being run over by a 
bus on my way to the grocery store. :)

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Jun 18 20:45:36 2005
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat, 18 Jun 2005 20:45:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Booting Linux from CD...
In-Reply-To: <42B427D6.1060206@axpr.net>
References: <1118966489.42b212d920fd2@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<42B427D6.1060206@axpr.net>
Message-ID: <1119152736.42b4ea6087873@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting AthlonRob <athlonrob at axpr.net>:

> plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> > I want to stay away from any Linux bootloader on the beginning 
> > of the drive to avoid a, giving it away that Linux or anything 
> > else is installed 
> 
> Ummm... why?

My friend wanted her XP system set up again.  She has limited 
computer literacy and malicious friends.  That's why the Linux 
system I set up on the other side of her drive doesn't need to 
be obvious.  I know traditionally that Linux is booted from a
program installed to the first hard drive.  For the 
purpose of simplicity and security though, I didn't want to do 
this.  She wanted XP set up again, she didn't need some 
strange boot program to figure out.  It would have been nice to 
use a second drive to back her Windows system up on, but that 
option wasn't available to me.  I don't think Linux will boot 
via lilo or grub anyways because if I remember right, I 
installed it to an extended partition.  My thing was to get 
XP to install correctly.  Trying to put a small Linux boot 
partition at the start of the drive always screwed XP up.  
I might have been able to trick the Windows partition into 
starting 50 megs into the drive via use of Linux fdisk, but 
that's just a guess on my part.  What I really want to set
her up with is a freedos bootable cd or a freedos bootable 
flash drive to start her Linux system with.  She has a bios
password, so she can lock her boot down.  I want to give her
a non obvious yet simple enough boot method that will allow
her to upgade her XP system to the NTFS filesystem protecting
it even better.  Considering my backup method is bit based,
I don't care what the filesystem is on the Windows partition.
For those wondering why I don't go to dos mode in Windows
XP, it seems to use a protected mode memory manager that
loadlin doesn't work with.  I gave her a Windows 98 system
disk with a batch file to launch loadlin, but she lost that.
I want to hand her a single cd or flash key that she can boot 
Linux with.  I backed her Windows system up before I went
online to activate it.  I added a ton of open source 
utilities and tools which are in the backup.  For those
who don't like to sit and answer Windows XP installation
prompts, telling Linux to restore Windows and walking out
for a few hours is far more appealing.  That's why I did
that.  Turns out, Fedora Core 3 can use all of her 
hardware, but I didn't emphasize this much to avoid
overwhelming her.

     Michael C. Robinson

> http://www.arkeia.com/
> 
> There is a free version that you can use in a single-server environment.
> 
> I have used Arkeia for several years in a variety of situations and am
> quite impressed by its power and flexibility.  It may take a little time
> to get your head around the software's functioning, but it is worth it.
> 
> There are some debates surrounding its authentication protocol, but in
> the situation you describe it doesn't sound like an issue.

> Michael

Sorry to reply to my own message but it appears that Arkeia changed
their terms for the free version since I last visited their site.  It
used to be limited by number of servers (max 1 for the free version)
whereas now their free version is only good for up to 50 GB.  Too bad.

For free/simple, I would agree with the people that said using rsync is
a good way to go.  Another option I use is Unison:

<http://www.cis.upenn.edu/~bcpierce/unison/>

I use it to keep my laptop and desktop in synch.  Unison expands upon
rsync by providing merge capabilities plus a GUI.

If you can mount the share locally, you don't need to run Unison on the
remote box.  If the remote share can not be mounted locally, Unison
supports other transport methods, but Unison must be available on the
remote machine.

Michael

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Jun 21 23:06:36 2005

In-Reply-To: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>

Message-ID: <1119420396.42b8ffec32167@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>:

> OK, at this point I have quite a bit of effort invested in configurations 
> on my Ubuntu-64 Compaq. I feel it's time to start making backups.
> 
> The backup medium will be an external USB drive connected to my 
> Windows 2000 computer. I created a share on the Windows 2000 
> computer and the Linux laptop sees the drive just fine. (Except it 
> asks for my password for the Windows computer each time I try to 
> access it the first time during a session, which may be a stumbling 
> block for unattended backups.)
> 
> I googled for "linux backup" and got all kinds of stuff. Almost all 
> was commercial software and designed for massive installations. 
> The rest seemed improper for one reason or another.
> 
> I could backup from my Windows 2000 computer, except I haven't 
> been able to get the Windows 2000 computer to see the drive on 
> the laptop. It sees the laptop, and when I click to browse it, 
> Windows pops up a login box. Un fortunately, when I enter my 
> username and password for the laptop, Windows keeps saying it is 
> wrong. I've tried a few things, like putting //laptopname/ in front of 
> my username, but nothing works. Anyway, it just seems more 
> logical to back up each computer from that computer. I'll deal with 
> the Windows network issue later.
> 
> So does anyone have any suggestions for simple backup solutions 
> that would work in my situation, and without requiring a degree in 
> CompSci to run it?



Freedos and msclient-3.0 might work.  I've network booted dos. 
Linux can be network booted and run root over network.  With NTFS 
I don't know, you might be in trouble there.  If, on the other 
hand, you figure out how to do a bit backup of your NTFS 
filesystem via dd, you should be fine.  Since freedos is GNU 
gpl'ed, I don't see any reason why someone couldn't port dd 
to it to create a simple partition imaging solution.  Something 
like menuconfig that works as a front end to dd could be quite 
nice.  One of the reasons I don't like anything that uses NTFS 
is the problem of backing it up.  It wouldn't be so bad if 
Windows XP, etc., didn't use drive letters and could support a 
mode where a fat partition is not a valid program installation 
target.  If you could install Freedos to that fat partition and 
boot it with msclient, that could work quite nicely.  Filesystem 
vagueness is a large concern of mine with modern versions of 
Windows.  It's a good reason to ask anyone who says they use Windows 
for this or that, what would it take to get you comfortable enough 
to abandon Microsoft software altogether?  The Windows filesystem 
should be based on an open public standard, but as far as I know, 
that's not the case.  I'd recommend a cheap PII if you're going to
have a backup server, just run Linux on it and add a promise hard 
card that there's kernel support for.  What you do is network export
via NFS a basic Linux filesystem and via a trivial ftp server 
an NFS root kernel also.  After that, you either use a floppy disk 
with etherboot or a PXE enabled network card.  I think even Redhat
7.3 would work if you're going the dd route, but I'd recommend
Fedora Core 3 or possibly even Slackware.

     Michael Robinson

From jemcevoy at gmail.com  Wed Jun 22 13:04:02 2005
From: jemcevoy at gmail.com (James McEvoy)
Date: Wed, 22 Jun 2005 13:04:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backups?
In-Reply-To: <1119420396.42b8ffec32167@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <42B7006B.9842.6C3578C8@localhost>
	<1119420396.42b8ffec32167@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <7e9d30dd0506221304218a8d6c@mail.gmail.com>

One way to get reliable access to your network Windows disk is to add
an entry to the /etc/fstab file and create a protected file named
/etc/smb.credentials to hold your windows username and password.

Add this entry to the /etc/fstab file:
   \\Win2k\usbDrive /usbDrive  smbfs 
credentials=/etc/smb.credentials,workgroup=HomeNet,user 0 0

Create /etc/smb.credentials as root with the following two lines and
chmod to 600:
   username = windowsLoginid
   password = windowsPassword

  chmod 600  /etc/smb.credentials
Then create the directoy on which to mount the usbDrive
  mkdir /usbDrive

Now you can mount the drive as follows and automatically at boot.
  mount /usbDrive


If you take the laptop off your home network add the ",noauto" option
to the /etc/fstab entry to avoid slow booting but you will need to
manually mount /usbDrive as root before use.

  --jim

# Samba config file created using SWAT
# from 127.0.0.1 (127.0.0.1)
# Date: 2005/06/21 12:00:09

# Global parameters
[global]
	netbios aliases = new
	server string = Samba-SUSE
	interfaces = eth0
	encrypt passwords = No
	min password length = 3
	map to guest = Bad User
	pam password change = Yes
	log level = 2
	server signing = auto
	printcap cache time = 750
	printcap name = cups
	logon path = \\%L\profiles\.msprofile
	logon drive = P:
	logon home = \\%L\%U\.9xprofile
	preferred master = No
	local master = No
	domain master = No
	ldap idmap suffix = ou=Idmap
	ldap machine suffix = ou=Computers
	ldap suffix = dc=example,dc=com
	ldap ssl = no
	printer admin = @ntadmin, root, administrator
	cups options = raw
	oplocks = No
	level2 oplocks = No
	include = /etc/samba/dhcp.conf

[homes]
	comment = Home Directory for : %U
	path = %H
	valid users = %U
	read only = No
	inherit acls = Yes

[quickbooks]
	path = /home/QuickBooks
	valid users = don, jim, steve, mike
	read list = don, jim, steve, mike
	write list = don, jim, steve, mike
	read only = No
	create mask = 0777
	hide special files = Yes
	hide unreadable = Yes

	path = /home/sys/
	valid users = don, jim, mike, deb, steve, brent, brighton
	write list = don, jim, mike, deb, steve, brent, brighton
	read only = No
	create mask = 0777
	guest ok = Yes
	case sensitive = No
	msdfs proxy = no

	read only = No
	guest ok = Yes

From drl at drloree.com  Wed Jun 22 23:21:32 2005
From: drl at drloree.com (Derek Loree)
Date: Wed, 22 Jun 2005 23:21:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] slackware an scsi error
In-Reply-To: <42B98AD2.30702@mideplan.go.cr>
References: <42B98AD2.30702@mideplan.go.cr>
Message-ID: <1119507692.1492.7.camel@fastlinux.homenet>

On Wed, 2005-06-22 at 09:59 -0600, "El?dier Moya R." wrote:
> Hello,
> 
> I'm trying to install slackware on my scsi hd. I had some problems with 
> that so I disable parity (on the scsi administration tool that come with 
> the disk) and everything seems to be ok.
> 
> Now I can see the disk from slackware installation (/dev/sda), the 
> problem is that when I use cfdisk to make partitions it can't save the 
> new partition scheme. No error messages, just when I run  cfdisk again 
> it says Do you wish to start with a zero table?

If there is no error, then more than likely it is doing what you ask it
to.  Some systems have to reboot after changing partition information,
this is usually indicated by a failure to re-read the partition table.
I would also recommend booting a live-CD distribution (like Knoppix --
it has pretty good hardware detection) and use that to change the
partition tables.

Good luck,


-- 
Derek Loree

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Jun 24 21:35:52 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)

>

> 
> Yet for some reason the Debian install can't see the hard drive.

How far does it get? Does it boot? Does it get to detecting hardware and
simply doesn't find the drive?

I think you pegged the most likely issue. Mainly many SATA controllers
don't look like normal IDE controllers. The first thing I can think of to
do is tell it to boot the 2.6.8 kernel (more recent drivers) rather than
the 2.4.27 kernel (the conservative default).

From nsxfreddy at verizon.net  Sat Jun 25 00:23:13 2005
From: nsxfreddy at verizon.net (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Sat, 25 Jun 2005 00:23:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Debian Install
In-Reply-To: <000001c57941$3fe710b0$6600000a@Alpha>
References: <000001c57941$3fe710b0$6600000a@Alpha>
Message-ID: <42BD0661.1040404@verizon.net>

> I think that I agree with you that the SATA controllers don't look like IDE
> controllers, yet I didn't see any detection for SATA drives going on during
> boot-up. OR did I miss something during bootup? Is there a Magical switch
> that I need to flag?

You can check in your BIOS, it's possible the drive has a "compatibility 
mode" or some such, which would allow you to install (but probably cost 
you in performance).  The drive would then show up as a PATA device 
instead.  Most likely if it's a really new machine the PCI IDs for the 
SATA controller aren't in the kernel yet so it won't recognize it. 
2.6.8 is from last August, which is ancient in driver/hardware time.  If 
you do an lspci do you get unknown devices?

Jason


From shadygrove at gmail.com  Sat Jun 25 09:38:42 2005
From: shadygrove at gmail.com (Andrew Becherer)
Date: Sat, 25 Jun 2005 09:38:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Debian Install
In-Reply-To: <000901c5792a$68cc8b70$6600000a@Alpha>
References: <000901c5792a$68cc8b70$6600000a@Alpha>
Message-ID: <42BD8892.40008@gmail.com>

Piet van Weel wrote:
> I've got a nice new DELL XPS2 Laptop. 
> 
> It comes with the SATA drive.
> 
>  
> 
> Yet for some reason the Debian install can't see the hard drive.
> 
> Any ideas?


My buddy Jeff put together this sweet Pentium M (Dothan) based dektop 
system. It is his intention to turn it into a MythTV box. His box has a 
Promise SATA controller (not sure exactly which one).

Initially we thought this would be no problem because the latest version 
of Knoppix booted and recognized the 200GB drive. We attempted to 
install FC3 first because we wanted to use Jarod Smith's howto/packages. 
But FC3 could not drive the sata controller. FC3 loaded the promise_sata 
module but the drive was not recognized. Ubuntu 5.04 was the same way. 
The commonality between Ubuntu and FC3 was a sata_promise kernel module 
older than version 1.01.

In the meanwhile Jeff decided to install Gentoo on that system. I don't 
recall reading which sata controller you have but if it is a promise 
this could be your problem.

Andrew Becherer

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jun 25 23:26:00 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 25 Jun 2005 23:26:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] A Broadband Beat-Down - article from NY Times
In-Reply-To: <1119749713.6995.3.camel@8qbcf21.site>
References: <1119749713.6995.3.camel@8qbcf21.site>
Message-ID: <20050626062600.GA2220@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sat, Jun 25, 2005 at 06:35:13PM -0700, Mel Andres wrote:
> http://www.nytimes.com/2005/06/25/technology/25online.ready.html?
> th&emc=th
> 
> Says Japan has more broadband, at lower prices, and 16 times the speed.
> Blames Bush administration for not keeping up.
> 
> Mel

Besides the obvious absurdity of expecting the Thug In Chief to know
what broadband is (he probably thinks "broadband" refers to the Dixie
Chicks, and is agin' 'em),  the NYT isn't thinking about what is
involved in providing broadband to a whole population.  Japan is a
tiny country, and most of the population lives stacked up 20 stories
high.  It is damned easy to provide broadband there.  

The US is 25 times bigger, with a much more varied population. 
We have single farms bigger than some of the Japanese islands.  In
our densest urban areas, I bet the broadband is just as good, but
very little of the US population lives at Japanese densities, and
it costs a lot to get broadband to our single-family-home suburbs.  

Another matter is one of simple demand.  If you ride a subway in
Tokyo, damned near everyone is online all the time, staring crosseyed
at their DoCoMo phones and wearing their thumbs out typing in messages.
A lot of their so-called "broadband infrastructure" is actually store-
and-forward messaging to those phones.  That may be "high bandwidth",
but isn't the same as a cable or fiber.  Also, the transportation is
so crowded there that it is much easier to "visit electronically" than
go from place to place like we do here.  From Hitachi Central Labs out
in the 'burbs, to Shinjuku (the western edge of the inner ring of Tokyo)
is two hours on the express subway.  Much easier to pick up a phone or
send an email, and download a movie rather than go to a video store.  

What can you do with disposable income in Tokyo?  The houses are tiny,
the streets are tiny, no room for cars or dishwashers or video
entertainment centers.  Folks there spend more money on computers and
less on "American" style goods.  So why not spend money on better
bandwidth, too?  A fiber connection is expensive, but it doesn't take
much room...

After you factor in all the geographic, demographic, economic, and
cultural differences between the countries, you will find that the
relative positions in broadband access are about what you would
expect them to be.  If we increased the US population to 4 billion,
or moved everybody in the US into California, Oregon, and Washington,
we would have much better broadband here, too.  If your broadband
sucks, move downtown.  If downtown sucks, move to the sticks and
use a modem.  Life is a tradeoff.

Still, if this is an excuse to dump on Bush, I'll go along. ;-)

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> Because, of course it's the government's job to furnish everybody with

Yes, it is. That's one of the reasons why I'm moving to Canada (our new 
house closes July 12).

Seems that 10 years ago, Canada made the commitment to wire EVERY 
community, no matter how remote - the program is called CANCAP, Canadian 
Community Access Project. At that time, they KNEW that they could not 
predict what technology would look like in 10 or 20 years. So they 
decided to wire everybody, rolling out the newest technologies as they 
came available.

By the end of 2006, EVERY community, including those little Inuiit 
villages north of the Arctic Circle, including those two or three houses 
clustered on some lake that can only be accessed by float plane, 
including every small town and every large city, will have broadband 
access. In the cities, its through the cable companies. In the villages, 
it is through 802.11b/g. And this Fall, they will start rolling out WiMax.

It's a matter of public policy. Something that is totally lacking in 
Second World Countries, like that United States. But then, that's 
contrary to the Libertarian/Christian Facist religion.

I know this should have been on PLUG-Talk, but I refuse to let little 
asides like this slip by. It is because we let neocon phrases and 
ideologies slip into our society's consciousness unchallenged, laughing 
at them as if they were harmless, that our whole society has gone to hell.

If have made the moral decision to challenge all Libertarian and Neocon 
statements at every opportunity, just as I do racist and homophobic 
speech. If you don't want me coming back at you strong on PLUG, only 
spew the Neocon/Libertarian ideology on PLUG-Talk.

Peace, Justice, and Socialism for All,
-Ron

I haven't ever been a big Debian fan, but we installed Ubuntu on a 
couple of boxes where I work and I liked it. I wanted to play with it 
more and did an install here at home, but the resolution in Gnome seems 
stuck at 640x480. When I go into the Gnome tool to change resolutions, 
it only shows that one on the list.

After some Googling I found the hint to try:

dpkg-reconfigure xserver-xorg

I did this and configured the server to only use 1024x768. I verified 
that the new configuration had been written to the xorg.conf 
(/etc/X11/xorg.conf IIRR), but when I rebooted I was still stuck in 
640x480 like before. After that I edited the xorg.conf so that only this 
existed in the Screen section:

Section "Screen"
         Identifier      "Default Screen"
         Device          "NVIDIA Corporation NV18 [GeForce4 MX - nForce 
GPU]"
         Monitor         "CPD-200ES"
         DefaultDepth    16
         SubSection "Display"
                 Depth           16
                 Modes           "1024x768"
         EndSubSection
EndSection

I rebooted and I was still in 640x480.

Any ideas? I've not run into this before...


Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sun Jun 26 13:43:41 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sun, 26 Jun 2005 13:43:41 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu X question
In-Reply-To: <42BF0C7D.6060100@paranoid.org>

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506261338110.28460@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sun, 26 Jun 2005, Rich Burroughs wrote:

>        DefaultDepth    16
>        SubSection "Display"
>                Depth           16
>                Modes           "1024x768"

> Any ideas? I've not run into this before...

Rich,

   Why a 16-bit color depth? With that video card, and screen resolution of
1024x768 you should easily support 24-bit colors (True Color). That's
essentially the same as 32-bit color.

   I've not had this problem before, but it may be a mis-match between the
color depth and the screen resolution.

   For comparison, here's the relevant sections of /etc/X11/xorg.conf on my
Slackware-10.1 system:

Section "Screen"
     Identifier  "Regular Screen"
     Device      "ATI Rage 128 Pro Ultra"
     Monitor     "Hyundai LCD"

# If your card can handle it, a higher default color depth (like 24 or 32)
# is highly recommended.

#   DefaultDepth 8
#   DefaultDepth 16
    DefaultDepth 24
#   DefaultDepth 32

     Subsection "Display"
         Depth       8
         Modes "1024x768" "800x600" "640x480"
     EndSubsection
     Subsection "Display"
         Depth       16
         Modes "1280x1024" "1024x768" "800x600"
     EndSubsection
     Subsection "Display"
         Depth       24
#        Modes "1280x960" "1024x768" "800x600"
         Modes "1280x1024" "1024x768"
     EndSubsection
     Subsection "Display"
         Depth       32
         Modes "1280x960" "1024x768" "800x600"
     EndSubsection

EndSection

Rich

From creswick at gmail.com  Sun Jun 26 14:54:39 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Sun, 26 Jun 2005 17:54:39 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu X question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506261338110.28460@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <42BF0C7D.6060100@paranoid.org>

Message-ID: <c834ec8050626145459228bc9@mail.gmail.com>

On 6/26/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Sun, 26 Jun 2005, Rich Burroughs wrote:
> 
> >        DefaultDepth    16
> >        SubSection "Display"
> >                Depth           16
> >                Modes           "1024x768"
> 
> > Any ideas? I've not run into this before...


I had this problem on Friday, it ended up that there were no
horizontal refresh / vert.sync lines for the monitor (I had switched
to an older, but nicer monitor, and it did not support EDIC, or
whatever the autodetection protocol is).

By specifying these setting in the Monitor section, the problem went
away, and X started obeying my settings in the Display and Screen
section(s).

Example settings: (don't use the numbers below unless you *know* they
are correct for you monitor, the example exists so you can spell the
option name correctly :)

HorizSync   31.5 - 50.0
VertRefresh  40-90

--Rogan

I have Red Hat 7.0 running on a machine with my largest hard drive. I run 
Samba on it, and have been using it regularly with my Winders machines 
(Win98), and can open files on it with Winders applications. I can also move 
files between it and my SuSE 9.1 machine using a Samba client on the SuSE 
machine.

I am trying to make a bigger break with Winders. I still need to be able to 
see and edit some MS Office files kept on the RH 7 machine, but I'd also like 
to be able to edit those same files using Open Office on the SuSE machine. 

It appears that Open Office doesn't interact with Samba. Am I missing 
something in Open Office, or do I need to set up a different network file 
sharing system between the RH machine and the SuSE machine, and if so, will 
it and Samba play nice together? I'm happy to read the documentation, but I 
don't know what system to read about.

Thanks for any advice.

From russj at dimstar.net  Sun Jun 26 17:10:19 2005
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Sun, 26 Jun 2005 17:10:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Accessing files on a local network
In-Reply-To: <200506261657.07589.rsteff@comcast.net>
References: <200506261657.07589.rsteff@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <42BF43EB.9060705@dimstar.net>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

>It appears that Open Office doesn't interact with Samba. Am I missing 
>something in Open Office, or do I need to set up a different network file 
>sharing system between the RH machine and the SuSE machine, and if so, will 
>it and Samba play nice together? I'm happy to read the documentation, but I 
>don't know what system to read about.
>  
>
The network transport should be transparent to OO. You need to mount the 
share prior to trying to open the file, but that's pretty much 
universal. You'd have to mount an NFS share before opening the file 
there as well.

So technically, you are right. OO doesn't interactive with Samba. It 
won't mount the share for you.

Once you have the share mounted, you should be able to open the file, 
assuming you have permission to read the file as the user you are 
running OO as.

--

> It appears that Open Office doesn't interact with Samba. Am I missing

> file sharing system between the RH machine and the SuSE machine, and
> if so, will it and Samba play nice together? I'm happy to read the
> documentation, but I don't know what system to read about.

I've been using OpenOffice.org 1.1.4 on my Windows machine for a 
long time. So when I got my new laptop and installed Linux on it, 
naturally I went for OpenOffice.org. At this time it has 1.1.3 
because it is running Ubuntu and that is all Ubuntu seems to offer. 
In fact, I just went through a lot of frustration trying to get the beta 
of 2.0 installed, and unsuccessfully.

The reason I wanted the beta of 2.0 is that I have been given to 
understand that it fixes a serious problem I am having with 1.1.3 on 
the Ubuntu machine. It seems that OpenOffice.org calls your 
default printer "Generic Printer," and doesn't seem to recognize 
much of its features. I have a big-ass networked Laserjet 5SiMx 
with all kinds of trays and options, most of which I would not be 
able to use, although all my other applications on the Ubuntu 
computer can print to it with the same ease that I can print to it 
from applications on the Windows computer. To solve this problem, 
OpenOffice.org comes with a GUI utility launched by spadmin, a 
script. Unfortunately, spadmin launches for me, but the minute I 
click on a button to do anything it disappears from my desktop.

Worse, I can't even print to the printer from OpenOffice.org on the 
Ubuntu computer because it sends the print job into outer space 
somewhere. The print job never gets to the printer. Again, no 
problem printing to the printer from any other program on the 
Ubuntu computer.

I wanted the beta of 2.0 because I am told that it fixes this problem 
completely. In 2.0 it just uses whatever printer is defined in CUPS 
and uses however it is set up there.

Now, I related this sad tale to you to point out that OpenOffice.org 
on Linux does some odd things. It does not play nicely in the Linux 
world. It seriously needs to work on sharing. Everyone else is 
happy to use stuff that is installed on the Linux computer, but it 
seems to think it needs to have its own personal version of 
everything, all to itself.

So it does not surprise me that it ignores Samba. I haven't tried to 
use Samba yet, as I had to give up on it for the time being. Until 
2.0 comes out and there is a debian package that I can install, I'm 
having to do my word processing on the Windows computer.

Just now I tried using OpenOffice.org Writer on the Ubuntu 
computer to open .sxw files on the Windows share. No go. Can't 
browse to it because all it sees is the Ubuntu filesystem. Then I 
tried opening a Nautilus window to display the Windows share, and 
then dragging and dropping the file on Writer. It showed a + in the 
mouse pointer, but when I dropped it, nothing happened.

So I guess I have no solution. But you might buzz off to 
openoffice.org and look through the feature list for 2.0 to see if it 
will fix this problem. That may be your only hope. Unless you want 
to drag the files to a folder on your Linux computer and open them 
from there, then copy them back to the Windows share.

:(

Are you sure the share is mounted? Usually that is a separate step. Nautilus 
lets you see the files in a Samba share without it being mounted.  Does it 
appear with the ls command? If ls does not see it, it is not mounted.

Can you right-click on the file in Nautilus and do a "open with..."? (I'm not 
a nautilus user) 

> 
> All other programs on the Linux machine have no problem seeing 
> the share. It's just OpenOffice.org. Well, OK, some can't see it in 
> their "Open file" dialog box, but I can drag and drop from a Nautilus 
> window onto the application and the file opens fine.
> 

I also use OO, and it sees Samba shares in the file open dialog if they are 
actually mounted.

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

>On Sunday 26 June 2005 5:10 pm, Russ Johnson wrote:
>
>  
>
>>The network transport should be transparent to OO. You need to mount the
>>share prior to trying to open the file, but that's pretty much
>>universal. You'd have to mount an NFS share before opening the file
>>there as well.
>>    
>>
>
>SuSE 9.1 provides desktop icons to start instances of Konqueror for local 
>system browsing (called My Computer) and for "Network Browsing." Using 
>Network browsing, I navigate to the directory containing the files on the RH 
>machine. I assume that this mounts that system on the SuSE machine, but OO 
>still does not see the network.
>
Probably not. This would be the same as browsing the samba shares from a 
Windows machine with the //machine/path/to/files path.

I'm not sure about OO, but many Linux apps do not support opening files 
this way. I recall a thread on here a few weeks back regarding K3b and 
opening an ISO. It didn't support file:/path/to/file conventions, and 
aborted on that user.

You'll probably have to issue a smbmount or mount -t smb command to 
mount the share on a mountpoint prior to trying to open the file.

>Clicking on the entry of the .doc file results in a dialog box 
>offering to open the file with OO writer. For some reason, it worked this 
>time. I guess I must not have followed that procedure the same way the last 
>time.
>  
>

That's a good sign. Maybe OO does support opening files via URI.

From russj at dimstar.net  Sun Jun 26 20:53:42 2005
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)

	<42BF0A4E.31243.15A0F3A7@localhost>
Message-ID: <42BF7846.9080302@dimstar.net>

John Jordan wrote:

>In the meantime, how come I can drag and drop a file from that 
>share (using Nautilus) onto a gedit window and gedit will open it? 
>Ditto for "open with" when I right-click on a file in the Nautilus 
>window. And I can drag and drop a file from the Nautilus window 
>onto the desktop or anyplace else on the Linux computer. 
>
>But if the destination is OpenOffice.org. Then it shows a + on the 
>mouse pointer, but nothing happens when I drop it.
>  
>
It appears OO doesn't support the path that your window manager is 
giving it. In my example earlier, k3b didn't support opening a file with 
the URI "file:/path/to/file". But it did support "/path/to/file".

If you can determine what URI nautilus is providing to OO, then maybe we 
can figure out if there's some other URI that will work. Maybe 
"smb://machine/path/to/file" will work if you put that in the file>open 
dialog.

If I remember correctly, you should be able to mount the remote file 
system with this command:

#mount -t smbfs -o username=windowsloginname:password=windowspassword 
machine:/path /mount/point

-- 
Russ Johnson

Subject: [PLUG] A Broadband Beat-Down - article from NY Times

On Sun, 26 Jun 2005, John Telford wrote:

> Hello
>
> Read the "Down to the Wire" Foreign Affairs article the NYT refers to
> at:
> http://www.foreignaffairs.org/20050501faessay84311/thomas-bleha/down-to-
> the-wire.html
>
> Read it and weep. The American Telecoms acting like Russian Polit
> Bureaus, with armies of lobbyist in Washington spreading truck loads of
> money, are defending their turf at any cost.  Satisfying greed
> immediately is blinding the Telecoms and Washington to the riches of the
> future.
>
Years ago, an Australian posted to one of the Usenet groups I was
following at the time the following rhetorical question: "Why is the
predominant telcommunications corporation of every country always
universally despised?"

People who advocate privitization of utilities (which is what the phone
companies are), argue that customers can't vote out the maangement of
public utilities. People who advocate public ownership of utilities
argue that they are immune to the effects of the marketplace.

Meanwhile, there is too little protection for the average customer from
mistreatment of an organization whose goals are often completely the
opposite -- whether owned by government or a number of investment firms.

Geoff

From: william.leake at gmail.com (William Leake)

In-Reply-To: <20050627005441.7246143C1@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050627005441.7246143C1@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <1119860553.21955.26.camel@localhost.localdomain>

John,

Have you had any luck getting OO 2.0 beta (1.9.79.2) installed?  I
installed it right from synaptic after adding the backports repository
to my /etc/apt/sources.list.  Here is a copy of mine in case it helps:

deb cdrom:[Ubuntu 5.04 _Hoary Hedgehog_ - Release i386 (20050407)]/
hoary main restricted
deb-src http://us.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary main restricted

## Major bug fix updates produced after the final release of the
## distribution.
deb http://us.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-updates main restricted
deb-src http://us.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-updates main
restricted

## Uncomment the following two lines to add software from the 'universe'
## repository.
## N.B. software from this repository is ENTIRELY UNSUPPORTED by the
Ubuntu
## team, and may not be under a free licence. Please satisfy yourself as
to
## your rights to use the software. Also, please note that software in
## universe WILL NOT receive any review or updates from the Ubuntu
security
## team.



## This line was added
deb http://ndiswrapper.sourceforge.net/debian ./

deb-src http://us.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary universe

deb http://security.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-security main restricted
deb-src http://security.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-security main restricted

deb http://security.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-security universe
deb-src http://security.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary-security universe

deb http://archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/ hoary multiverse universe main
restricted
deb-src http://archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu hoary multiverse


## UBUNTU BACKPORTS -- I added these two
deb http://ubuntu-backports.mirrormax.net/ hoary-backports main universe
multiverse restricted
deb http://ubuntu-backports.mirrormax.net/ hoary-extras main universe
multiverse restricted

=======================EOF===============================
The backports repo gives some nice updates.  I have the OO beta and gaim
1.3.1. Both from the comfort of synaptic.  Yes, I do know how to use the
CL - but that sure is not the easiest way anymore.

HTH,
Reid






From g.c.anderson at verizon.net  Mon Jun 27 07:01:56 2005
From: g.c.anderson at verizon.net (Glenn Anderson)
Date: Mon, 27 Jun 2005 07:01:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Newbie needs help
Message-ID: <01b401c57b20$c7d9b230$2f01a8c0@dads>

After a lot of blind alleys I finally got my system up.  I suspected a flaky video card (ATI Rage Pro 32M AGP) or hosed AGP support so I dusted off an old S3 PCI video card and gave it a shot- XFree came up with 800x600 resolution (yuk).  I reinserted my ATI card, re-diddled xf86config  and got a lot of complaints about no screen found, etc.  I noticed that the configuration file doing the complaining was XFree86Config-4, so I took a look and found some old settings I hadn't made for some time.  I got adventurous and changed my video driver to "r128" form the "s3virge" that was in the file and entered the higher screen resolutions, rebooted, and there it was.

Now I get to futz with my sound card settings and try to get a reliable home network connection to my other PCs.  This is fun when it finally works.

Side note: I'll stay with X-windows just for the screensavers. Even my 14-year-old likes them.

Pop "greylisting" into Google. Wil's choice to use it is, to my mind, a

annoying delays.

On 6/27/05 1:53 PM, Russ Johnson wrote:

> It appeared to me that I was blocked from sending, and for that I wanted 
> to know what I'd done to deserve that. I don't remember being involved 
> in a flame war lately... :)

Greylisting is not (usually) discriminate. It has nothing to do with the 
content of your messages, past or present. It works on the assumption 
that spam runs won't even try to follow up on deferred messages, while 
legitimate MTAs will try again in a few minutes.

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Mon Jun 27 19:06:30 2005

Rich Burroughs wrote:
> I want the second interface to be transparent, like a bridge. In other
> words, it will have no IP address bound to it, it will just grab the
> traffic off the wire in stealth mode.

You realize if you're on a switched network nothing will come to that
second interface without arp poisoning, right?

What you're describing, though, is promiscuous mode, I believe.  It's
what I use on my VPN/bridge deployments to get the interfaces to accept
all packets sent to them (and send it on to the bridging magic).

man iptables has the details.

Rob

From nsxfreddy at verizon.net  Mon Jun 27 19:40:26 2005
From: nsxfreddy at verizon.net (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Mon, 27 Jun 2005 19:40:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Stupid(?) Snort question
In-Reply-To: <42C0A88B.9020901@paranoid.org>
References: <42C0A88B.9020901@paranoid.org>
Message-ID: <42C0B89A.70707@verizon.net>

Rich Burroughs wrote:
> I want to run Snort on a box with multiple interfaces. The first 
> interface will be used for administration, and the second will plug into 
> the network I want to sniff.
> 
> I want the second interface to be transparent, like a bridge. In other 
> words, it will have no IP address bound to it, it will just grab the 
> traffic off the wire in stealth mode.
> 
> I've used Snort before, but never with an interface that did not have an 
> IP bound to it. What do I need to do to configure that second interface 
> so that Snort can listen on it, without assigning it an IP? What 
> ifconfig command would set it up to work that way? This is a Linux box.

As someone else pointed out, if you are on a switch that doesn't have 
port mirroring/spanning you won't get much traffic without an arp 
poisoning attack.

That said, it is probably easiest to configure the interface as you 
would any other interface, except assign 0.0.0.0 to it.  Manually, do 
ifconfig ethX 0.0.0.0 up.  On RedHatish systems set 
/etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-ethX to have IPADDR=0.0.0.0, and 
just for kicks you can throw in PROMISC=yes.  Setting promiscuous mode 
explicitly isn't really needed on Linux though, as Snort should be able 
to set it for you (either by default or through a command-line option).

Also, be sure to feed Snort the command-line option to tell it which 
interface to use, or it might pick the wrong one.

Jason

> As someone else pointed out, if you are on a switch that doesn't have

On Mon, Jun 27, 2005 at 01:56:42PM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   Does anyone know how to change the color of the firefox vertical scroll 
>   bar
> "thumb?" It's a very light grey (as is the background of the window, but 
> this
> is not controllable by the window manager under X) and very difficult to see
> adjacent to the white background of the application window.
> 
>   Running qtconfig also seems to have no effect.
> 

It's a feature of the theme you're using for firefox. You can go to 
Tools -> Themes to choose another one.  (If there's only one listed
click on 'Get More Themes' to get a list of way too many to choose from.)

And if you're really gung ho, you can go to 

  http://kb.mozillazine.org/Theme_development

to learn how to create your own theme.

***Assembling Panoramic Photos: A Designer's Notebook
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596009755
Reflecting the very best of French photography, graphic design, and
digital artistry, this book is dazzling in concept and design. Part art
book, part how-to guide, this Designer's Notebook sits you down next to
renowned digital artists as they work step-by-step to create high-quality
panoramas and 360-degree virtual reality scenes. It's a goldmine for any
digital artist who wants to learn new Photoshop techniques for maximum
effect.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/assemblingadn/ 

Chapter 6, "On the Boulevards," is available online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/assemblingadn/chapter/ch06.pdf


***Ending Spam
Publisher: No Starch
ISBN: 1593270526
This landmark title describes, in-depth, how statistical filtering is
being used by next generation spam filters to identify and filter spam.
Readers gain a complete understanding of the mathematical approaches used
in today's spam filters, decoding, tokenization, the use of various
algorithms (including Bayesian analysis and Markovian discrimination), and
the benefits of using open-source solutions to end spam.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1593270526/


***Toad Pocket Reference for Oracle, 2nd Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596009712
This new edition, revised to reflect Toad for Oracle V8.0 and V8.5, is
packed with quick-reference material. It covers new features (e.g.,
CodeXpert and Script Debugger), shortcut keys, productivity tips, and
more. If you're a developer needing to streamline code development or a
DBA trying to simplify day-to-day administrative tasks, this pocket-sized
guide is the one book to have by your side.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/toadpr2/


***Mac OS X Tiger for Unix Geeks
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596009127
This is the book for Mac command-line fans. Completely revised and updated
to cover Mac OS X Tiger, this invaluable resource helps you quickly and
painlessly get acclimated with Tiger's familiar-yet-foreign Unix
environment. If you're a Unix geek with an interest in Mac OS X, you'll
find that this concise book is the ideal survival guide for taming the
Unix side of Tiger.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/macxtigerunix/ 

Chapter 2, "Searching and Metadata," is available online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/macxtigerunix/chapter/index.html


***Firefox Secrets
Publisher: SitePoint
ISBN: 0975240242
"Firefox Secrets" is a guide to getting the most from the Mozilla Firefox
browser. It will teach you to use all the hidden features, extensions, and
tricks available to Firefox. This book is loaded with tips and advice on
everything from making your browser faster to essential tools for
developers, including plenty of examples throughout.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/0975240242/


***Ship It!
Publisher: Pragmatic
ISBN: 0974514047
Aimed at beginning to intermediate programmers, this book provides a
wealth of practical advice on how to ensure successful software projects.
It begins by introducing the common technical infrastructure that every
project needs, letting readers choose from a variety of recommended
technologies according to their skills and budgets. The next sections
outline the necessary steps to get software out the door reliably, using
easy-to-adopt, best-of-breed practices that actually work.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/0974514047/


***eBay Hacks, 2nd Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0-596-10068-X
This second edition has been completely revised and updated with 30 brand
new hacks, plus tons of expanded, deepened, or otherwise completely
rewritten hacks. Learn clever tricks and shortcuts, such as advanced
searching techniques, selling strategies, photography tips, and even
research techniques for PowerSellers. This bestseller supplies you with
the tools you need to master eBay, whether as a buyer or seller, casual
surfer or serious collector, novice or seasoned expert.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/ebayhks2/

http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/ebayhks2/chapter/index.html


***Digital Video Hacks
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596009461
>From acquiring footage, mixing, editing, and adding effects to final
distribution, this book provides unique tips, tools, and techniques for

http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/digitalvideohks/


***Essential Mac OS X Panther Server Administration
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596006357
If you've ever wondered how to safely manipulate Mac OS X Panther Server's
many underlying configuration files, this book's for you. Full of
much-needed insight, clear explanations, troubleshooting tips, and
security information in every chapter, this comprehensive guide shows
system administrators how to utilize and customize the software's
capabilities and features for their individual needs. Topics covered
include installation, deployment, server management, web application
services, data gathering, and more.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/macxserver/


***.NET Gotchas
